### Post by deadlikeoscar on 2007-06-02
If you want to properly compare the cost of an Ubuntu system to a Vista one you need to remember that Microsoft Office and Adobe Photoshop (or whatever) cost money. If Open Office and the GIMP provide you with the functionality that you need (which is true for most people) then you have no reason to fork over a bunch of money for software--software you pretty much need. I know you can get open source software for Windows as well, but my point is that most people don't realize it when they order a Dell system. Even if they don't want to work with photos now, the possibility that, in the future, they may will often times justify the extra cost in their minds.

### Post by BionicBigfoot on 2007-06-02
I think that DELL did do something right. I agree with another post that graphic card drivers could end up being more stable if a big manufacturer like DELL is involved. In addition, I think M$ is getting worried. What with Microsoft wanting all of the sudden wanting Royalties from Linux and Open Source "Patent Infringements"  [http://dailytech.com/article.aspx?newsid=7269](http://dailytech.com/article.aspx?newsid=7269)

I also think that it is a tiny step for gaming companies to realize that there really is a an audience of Linux users and more people to market their games towards. Meaning that Open-GL (or another open source platform) might start giving Direct X 10 some serious competition.

Anyway, I just overall feel it is a step (albeit a tiny one) in the right direction.

PS: I don't really like Dell based on some bad experience personally. And when I was working as an A+ technician a few years ago, I noticed that DELL pc's were the most frequent pc's we had to repair.

### Post by dorsett on 2007-06-03
I configured two Dell inspiron 1505 notebooks with the exact same equipment, but one had Vista Home Basic and one had ubuntu, they came out to be the exact same price. Dell is ripping ubuntu users off. Why not just buy the Vista machine, install Ubuntu and ebay your legal copy of Vista. The fact that you can not switch between ubuntu and vista in their customization options (You have to start with either a Ubuntu or Vista machine) should tip people off that something is fishy. What would we be paying the extra $100 for that Dell is saving by not giving you a Vista copy? 

Another sneaky thing is that the Ubuntu machine starts at $599, while the Vista machine starts at $699, making you think you are saving a $100, but when you try to make them equivalent like I have shown below, they become the same price. Scandalous.

Dell Inspiron 1505

**$699 Vista (if I chose Intel Pro Wireless(only ubunut option) _becomes $728_)**

 1GB Shared Dual Channel DDR2 SDRAM at 533MHz, 2 Dimm [Included in Price] 
 256MB NVIDIA® GeForce® Go 7300 TurboCache&#8482; [Included in Price]

 Dell Wireless 1390b/g (54Mbps) [Included in Price] 
 Intel PRO/Wireless 3945a/g [add $29 or $1/month1] 

**$599 Ubuntu (upgrading to equivalent RAM & Video _becomes $728_)**

 512MB Shared Dual Channel DDR2 SDRAM at 533MHz, 2 DIMM [Included in Price] 
 1GB Shared Dual Channel DDR2 SDRAM at 533MHz, 2 Dimm [add $50 or $2/month1] 

 Intel® Graphics Media Accelerator 950 [Included in Price] 
 256MB NVIDIA® GeForce® Go 7300 TurboCache&#8482; [add $79 or $2/month1] 

 Intel PRO/Wireless 3945a/g [Included in Price]

### Post by dorsett on 2007-06-03
Here are the links I started with on two Inspiron E1505 machines. All the options that are missing from the first post had identical defaults for both machines.

I started with a Ubunutu $599 E1505N here:
[http://configure.us.dell.com/dellstore/config.aspx?c=us&cs=19&kc=6V440&l=en&oc=DNCWEL1&s=dhs](http://configure.us.dell.com/dellstore/config.aspx?c=us&cs=19&kc=6V440&l=en&oc=DNCWEL1&s=dhs)

and a Vista $699 E1505 here:
[http://configure.us.dell.com/dellstore/config.aspx?c=us&cs=19&l=en&oc=DNCWEA1&s=dhs](http://configure.us.dell.com/dellstore/config.aspx?c=us&cs=19&l=en&oc=DNCWEA1&s=dhs)

Not sure how long the links will last. They change prices and deals all the time. 

The two systems were identical as far as I could tell with these options
Intel® Pentium® dual-core T2080(1MB Cache/1.73GHz/533MHz FSB
Genuine Windows Vista™ Home Basic
15.4 inch Wide Screen XGA Display
1GB Shared Dual Channel DDR2 SDRAM at 533MHz, 2 Dimm
80GB SATA Hard Drive (5400RPM)
24X CD Burner/DVD Combo Drive
256MB ATI MOBILITY™ RADEON® X1400 HyperMemory™
Integrated Audio

I understand that ubuntu doesn't need as many resources and that Dell makes money installing random programs on a Vista machine. I'm just saying if you want to use Ubuntu, buy the vista machine, sell your Vista license and install Ubunutu yourself. You'll have a better laptop for the same amount of money. Of course, there was no option to downgrade the Vista machine's video card

### Post by my_key on 2007-06-06
Haha! Dell is offering us linux computers and what do we do? Complain about the price. This is priceless... :D

Look: to start up an new product line is sometimes quite costly. Prices will drop over time. Thats the way it works. (By the way: the issue seems to have already been resolved - or never occurred? - as some previous posts pointed out and i have experienced by trying just recently.)

Just buy the Ubuntu pc. This makes them show their is a market for linux pc's. If everybody buys the vista crap, Dell will have an excuse to drop the product line, "because there is no market for it".

Second of all: free software never was about price, it always has been about freedom. How much is that worth to you? We should support the vendors that are selling/supporting free software, so that our beloved free system gets more widely adopted. Maybe one day we will be able to walk up to just about any computer and use our favourite software. We are already starting to see free systems in schools, courts, ... (at least here in Belgium). And I believe that part of that success is because large companies like HP, Sun, Intel, Dell, and the like are openly expressing their support for gnu/linux as a powerful, stable and secure operating system.

I'm putting my money were my interests are: my freedom. And that means most likely my next system will be a dell.

peace,

Michael

### Post by jaybuntu on 2007-06-16
I've been using Ubuntu for a few months now first on an older IBM T30 notebook with 512MB RAM.  I was impressed that the performance of that machine was equivalent or better than my Dell Optiplex 720 with 2GB RAM and a dual core processor.  As fate would have it, the screen on my T30 went bad in the middle of May so I decided I liked the Idea of a new Dell notebook with Ubuntu preinstalled.  I got a great machine with some nice upgrades for under $800 (with a $300 off coupon code I found online) and was REALLY impressed with the performance and ease of use.

There were always a few quirky things with advanced graphics and hibernation on the T30 but these are completely gone on the E1505N.  The media controls and everything else "just works" the way it should.  And my 5 month old Dell Optiplex running Windows can't even hold a candle to this machine performance wise.  As an added plus, nearly every time I needed to do something the software was already installed.  

You can buy a machine with Vista and dual boot with Ubuntu if you want, but I doubt it will be quite as seamless.  On top of that, most of the people I know that tried Vista have gone back to WIndows XP.  Take the plunge - get Ubuntu preinstalled.  You won't be sorry.

---

### Post by steveneddy on 2007-06-17
> **Trixsey said:**
> Yeah but the question is why is Vista only 50$ more? Usually Vista is like 500$ or more, and Ubuntu is 0$. Shouldn't one be able to expect a price cut relative to the decreased cost?

A full version of Vista with packaging and license is $500, but if you have a lot of licenses, then you are installing from an image, so all you really have to charge for is the license, so that is where the difference is. 

I believe they give the manufacturer a disc image, or they make their own after testing and driver installation and then load it onto the HD at the factory. They don't crack open a new Vista box for every install, they just copy the HD image to a new machine, ghosting I believe it is called.

This is how network admins install OS's and programs system wide with 100's of PC's. It would take eons to install Windows on 200 PCs one at a time when they can set up an image install which takes only minutes for however many PCs you have to work with.

 This I believe is the difference in price. the hardware is still expensive.

Dell buys one CD or image and 100's if licenses, they install the image and sell the license, which is cheaper than a full on packaged CD.

---

### Post by Trixsey on 2007-06-17

Somehow though I got the impression the paper box and the CD weren't worth more than a few dollars though, and that the license itself was what made up for 99% of the cost.

### Post by HotShotDJ on 2007-06-17
[OEM]("http://arstechnica.com/news.ars/post/20070130-8730.html") versions of Windows Vista are much cheaper than purchasing the retail box.  On top of that, large OEM's such as Dell get a substantial discount from Microsoft, such that the $50 price difference between Ubuntu and Vista machines is probably about right.  Keep in mind also that Windows machines come with all kinds of subsidized trialware (aka crapware) that companies like AOL, Earthlink and McAfee pay Dell to install.  So, on an Ubuntu machine, that subsidization is lost.

Therefore, the idea that a Dell machine with Ubuntu should cost DELL_WITH_WINDOWS - WINDOWS_RETAIL_BOX = DELL_WITH_UBUNTU is false reasoning.

### Post by whistle on 2007-06-17
I customized two E1505's, one with Ubuntu and one with Home Basic.  The Ubuntu came out to be around 1320 before my $500 coupon and the Vista was around 1420 before the coupon.  However, the coupon only applies to 1399+, so I got a dock and both were the same price.

The VIsta came with the X1400 and the Ubuntu came with the Go 7300, but I wanted the 7300 anyways b/c of better Linux compatability, and the only game I play is a six year old OpenGL based one.  The Ubuntu had 512 MB of memory, while the Vista had 1 GB (2 dimms) - but I got two gigs off newegg, so the less memory that came with it, the better.

If you end up really missing Vista or XP in a few months, you can always get an OEM off newegg or ebay for 80 bucks... and if you're upgrading your memory yourself like me, that still saves you twenty bucks.

So I'd say the N is a better deal, and that's what I ended up buying... it's supposed to come in tomorrow or Tuesday :D yay!

Yes, and no.  If you tell them you've installed Ubuntu on it, they'll tell you it's not supported, and all but demand that you reinstall Windows (if only to run further diagnostics) before they will assist you.

### Post by neptho on 2007-07-06
> **Death_Sargent said:**
> That being said you may wan't to first look into power management options before even considering windows as anything sub vista does not have decent built in power support

I have to say that this is not true.  Dell's (own proprietary) system for XP works just fine; you can have it set HD spindown, lower the video, change timeouts, etc - nearly exactly to the way you can with ACPI in Linux.

However, XP doesn't have to worry about a 'tight polling' kernel, so you might even get a bit more life out of it than you would standard with Linux (at least with few applications running, and until programs stop using a polling based system instead of an event based system.  Kernel 2.6.21+ will help us whenever we get that standardized, but many of our favorite pieces of software are still going to be lingering on for a little bit before they're updated to take advantage of this).

   1049$

### Post by Dragonbite on 2007-07-09
The price differences should be very low, even if Ubuntu still costs more.  Dell is in the business to make money. period.  So they will price their products at whatever the market is willing to pay.

Yes, the OS is free, but the paychecks for the customer service whom had to be trained and the cost of the (little) marketing material created are not.

Anything that is "new" in the market is going to cost more so that the company can try and regain some of the initial cost of offering the new product, it's just the way business goes.  But all-in-all, Dell should not be pushing THAT large of a difference between systems (they can afford to have a little of the profit they make from the Windows machine go towards Ubuntu/Linux marketing).

Hope there is some sort of error in either the calculation or the different components.

### Post by octopuskevin on 2007-07-09
....umm maybe I am mistaken here, but I though that tech support for Ubuntu computers would be directly forwarded to Canonical...

While it is nice to see that Dell has begun to sell Ubuntu computers, It have seen several downsides to it...

1. price issues that people are talking about... inconsistancies in dell are not uncommon.. why is it that the price of a bluetooth adapter varies significantly depending on the computer you are customizing? why are there no 'standardized' prices for components? As such, why are Ubuntu computers more expensive then Windows? why are there no 'freebie upgrade' that are offered for Windows computers?

2. I am fairly certain that with the roll-out of Ubuntu computers, there will be greater emphesis on attempting to stop customers from getting windows refund requests.


My biggest peeve is that with Dell, and this probably goes with all OEM's out there, windows refund prices vary big time.. for example.. check out all the posts of people that DID get money back for their vista.. compare the prices of the guy from Germany to the guy from England.. how about the American?... strangely there are all different. When I started harrassing Dell several weeks ago, I was finally able to get a response from them.. they told me that in Canada I will only get $25 for a refund, regardless of the windows type... and that they would not offer a refund for 'extra's' such as systemworks...

SOoo What I see here is a bigger problem. OEM's like Dell are reluctant to tell customers exactly the value of a windows OEM, and are just as reluctant to offer these refunds. 

However I have a better idea...  Instead of arguing about why Dell does not offer Ubuntu computers in your nation, or that Linux users are getting screwed price wise... no, rather start sending nice happy cheerful emails out to inquire on how you can get your Vista refund: the Eula says so, and so does Dell's policies on sales.

Hence, while if we buy a lot of Ubuntu computers that nice and all, but it means that Linux users will still be restricted to a market dictated to us by these corporations. This is why I encourage everyone that is upset to do yourself, and the rest of the community a favour, and show these corporations that customer choices are required. That would have a far greater effect then just complaining on this forum.


Sorry if I'm ranting here, but I have been getting more and more angered by the fact that getting appropriate answers by Dell has been as hard as getting a pig to jump through hoops on fire. 

- Kevin

### Post by x86asm on 2007-07-09
You can see more about this topic from the source of the article here...

[http://www.devside.net/blog/](http://www.devside.net/blog/)

Dell streams all of its profits on commodity hardware boxes from the Windows OS. Dell can never truly price a commodity Ubuntu box below the Windows version, minus whatever publicity Ubuntu generates for them via driving more traffic towards further Windows based system sales.

Take away Windows, any you take away whatever profit margin they have.

And even if they could price Ubuntu systems lower, Microsoft makes up such a large percentage of their net profit margin, that even a slight increase in the Windows license price, or a slight decrease in what Microsoft "contributes" to Dell in the form of marketing kickbacks, or a fluctuation in any number of other background deals we know nothing about, would set Dell in a downward spiral and eventually ruin the company...

Dell hardly makes anything on each dollar of revenue, and other OEMs like HP are doing much better.

You could probably go as far as saying that Dell is nothing but an extension of Microsoft.

This has all been one big scam to sell more Windows Licenses.

### Post by putz3000 on 2007-07-10
Actually it is not surprising at all and probably does cost Dell more money.  Here is why:  MS throws kick back cash to company’s selling windows based machines.  So does Intel which is why for so many years Dell had been locked into Intel only chips.  Intel gave them money so long as they never sold an AMD system.  This is also why their stock value took a big hit when they added AMD chips to their lineup.  They lost money when they were already hurting.  They also lost some pricing advantage from Intel.

One of the reasons the Linux community has never been able to purchase Linux laptops and desktops without paying for a copy of windows is the same reason.  the profit margin on hardware these days is very, very low and a large percentage of a company’s profits come from MS kickbacks as long as they refuse to sell anything but windows based pc's and laptops.  By selling Linux machines, Dell is not going to get some of that MS money that it needs so, the Linux purchaser has to make up the difference.  Also, it is a very small percentage of Dells customer base that will request a Linux computer so there is no real volume in selling to the Linux market compared to windows.  

If anything, the price of the Linux computer is probably a reflection of the hardware’s true cost.  This is partially the reason Apple computers are higher than their windows competitors.

---

### Post by x86asm on 2007-07-10
> **WebDrake said:**
> Great.  First major retailer to ship Linux in a major way and you suggest we should boycott them

Dell started selling Red Hat systems in 2000 on a wide range of servers, desktop, and notebooks. WalMart was selling Linux systems in 2002. HP started playing with Linux in 2003. So this is nothing new.

And this current issue is about questioning if Dell is serious about its Linux line, or is just using Linux for publicity. 

We already know that Dell makes most of the profit seen on a system from Microsoft. So how are they going to make money selling hardware with no OS or Linux pre-installed?

### Post by az on 2007-07-10
Forgive me if I only skimmed through this thread.  I think people are overly excited about this.

Dell has sales.  They put stuff on sale when they think it will sell and that will make them a profit.  Sometimes they have a sale to undercut competition, sometimes it's just because they haven't moved enough stock.

I'm sure that in a few weeks, the afformentioned system will go back to regular price and the price difference between the Ubuntu system and Windows system will go back to what it was at the beginning.

As well, you can't assume that an Ubuntu computer costs Dell less than a Windows one.  Ubuntu surely costs them more in support than windows and whenever they sell a windows box, they make more profit from the OS sale - a profit they don't make from shipping Ubuntu.

### Post by darkog on 2007-07-10
> **kamaboko said:**
> I'm seem to recall posts from the past where people indicated they'd buy a Dell Ubuntu machine at any cost; anything to avoid MS.  Buy up or Dell will pull the plug on it.

Agreed. Buy up or shut up.

So, if no one buys a Ubu system from Dell, Dell is just going to kill the program, and there will be a big news release in the internet media, "No one wants Linux -- it's dead! Dell tried and failed, all people want to buy is Windows Vista". And MS will use that in their FUD campaigns and everything is right back to where it has always been and you will have no progress over a Windows OS monopoly. And you will be no closer to hardware vendors offering you supported Linux drivers for their new hardware -- because they will continue to think it's not worth their time. 

Don't buy pre-installed windows. Just wait for deals, look for coupons, and get a good deal on a Ubu Dell if you are going to buy from Dell. Your purchase counts and it might lead to better things in the future. And even if there is a small difference in final price or perhaps the hardware is not EXACTLY as the windows one with the "free upgrades"  -- don't be a cheap skate. Buy a Ubu Dell and make your purchase count.Think of it as your own personal contribution to the project in exchange for the free OS, stability, apps, and patches that you get for free.

Just my 2 cents.

### Post by aysiu on 2007-07-10
People like to play the "compare equivalent systems" game. It's fun. It brings up some pretty interesting debates, especially [in Mac/Windows PC comparisons.](http://ubuntucat.wordpress.com/2007/06/24/the-price-of-apples-2/)

But, in the end, unless you already desire a high-end system, it's just an intellectual exercise.

The truth is that a lot of people might want the default specs for the Ubuntu laptop and not necessarily need 2 GB of RAM and a 160 GB hard drive. If that's the case, you are paying less money, and that's all that matters.

It's the same reason Mac/Windows laptop comparisons make no sense. You may spec out the Windows laptop to be $100 more than the Mac laptop that has the same specifications, but the person who is inclined to buy a $600 laptop doesn't want those high-end specs. All she wants is to email, word process, and surf the web... maybe listen to music and organize family photos.

I don't see what there is to debate about here, frankly. If you don't want to buy a Dell Ubuntu laptop, don't buy it. If you do, buy it. It's your money. Choose for yourself what you want to do with it.

---

> People like to play the "compare equivalent systems" game. It's fun. It brings up some pretty interesting debates, especially in Mac/Windows PC comparisons.

The truth is that a lot of people might want the default specs for the Ubuntu laptop and not necessarily need 2 GB of RAM and a 160 GB hard drive. If that's the case, you are paying less money, and that's all that matters.

But those same people might be getting better hardware for the same price otherwise. Sure, anyone can freely make a dumb choice, but that doesn't make it less dumb.

### Post by WebDrake on 2007-07-10
> **x86asm said:**
> Dell started selling Red Hat systems in 2000 on a wide range of servers, desktop, and notebooks. WalMart was selling Linux systems in 2002. HP started playing with Linux in 2003. So this is nothing new.

And this current issue is about questioning if Dell is serious about its Linux line, or is just using Linux for publicity. 

We already know that Dell makes most of the profit seen on a system from Microsoft. So how are they going to make money selling hardware with no OS or Linux pre-installed?

I'm aware of all the examples you cite.  The present situation is still unprecedented for Linux in terms of both publicity and availability.  It's also significant in terms of support for a community-developed, free-as-in-freedom-oriented distro rather than an enterprise distro from a commercial seller.

Even if Dell is not serious now, it can be *made* serious very quickly if this product line is a success.  But serious or no, they won't be able to continue to support a product line that is not making money.  To follow such a high-profile announcement of adoption with an admission of failure would be a major blow to Linux everywhere, "proving" to computing manufacturers that it's not a viable operating system for general consumption.

Accusations of bad faith are just divisive and unhelpful---and as other posts in this thread have revealed, unfair and unfounded too.

---

-- Windows computers come with all sorts of crapware (free trials and such), which lower the price.
-- Microsoft probably gives Dell bulk discounts on Windows licenses.
-- People still want to compare similar specs in order to be outraged, but sometimes consumers don't want similar specs--they want cheap with lower specs, not relatively cheap for the value for higher specs.
-- If you're outraged, don't buy a Dell. If other people want to buy a Dell, leave them alone. Live and let live.

### Post by Evan Burchard on 2007-07-11
I bought a Z61m thinkpad from Staples a couple of months ago.  I looked online for one without windows, but it was cheaper than any of the ones preloaded with linux.  I tried vista, and thought that it was complicated in the wrong ways.  For me, ubuntu is complicated in the right ways.

I think that everyone's suprise at the linux version being more expensive, or not having the same promotions as microsoft.  I say, "so what."  Everyone is just disappointed that dell is not doing something that seems instictively right.  
aka. This is the way things should be in terms of money:

Free OS < Expensive OS.

But it's like this:

Free OS > Expensive OS - Probability of Buying MS Office - Bloatware - probably some other tricks I don't know

No system preloaded with linux before was cheaper than the microsoft version, and that is not changing with Dell.  Okay... so Dell just adds another resource to buy a more expensive linux system.  They are not offering a new service... they are getting publicity, and working the classic bait and switch with many I'm sure.  Well, good for them.  They're doing that, and at the same time promoting Ubuntu.  Possibly misrepresenting it a bit, but for people who might think that a computer IS windows (or that email, yahoo, google, etc. IS the internet), linux gets a little exposure, which isn't a bad thing.  A big name like Dell backing ubuntu (even if for their own purposes) might not seem important to those power users who can build their own pcs and are disenchanted with the company for one reason or another, but for Joe Dell customer, it might mean something that there IS an alternative to windows.  I would like to think that this could inspire someone, or at least make them a little curious.

As far as getting a cheaper machine by buying it preloaded with windows... once again... "so what?"  Yes, it would be nice to buy a machine without considering your effect on the linux community, and every corporation involved in your purchase.  But I would just advise that you ignore that and buy the windows version.  If you can get a refund by not signing the contract, well then sweet!  Free Money!!!  But I would argue that just buying the machines with windows is tantamount to free money.  I know linux isn't just about free beer... but think about it... you could save $200 dollars and get free shipping.  All you have to do is take what... 10 minutes to take off windows?  For me, I see that as a great opportunity, and completely in the spirit of all things linux.  ie (no pun intended) You are saving money and customizing your system.  

You don't deserve a refund for not using vista, because you already got a discount based on the assumption that you would play the game:  buy ms office, use google desktop, use the preloaded antivirus software (and update it), and above all, that you would not be educated enough to make a concious choice next time you want to buy a pc (and consequently buy windows).

Utimately, I see it like this.  If windows/antivirus/whatever companies want to sponsor part of my computer, with the misguided hope that I will buy their products or look at their ads, then good on them.  They saved me money.  And I think that by being involved in this community (fledgling as I am), I show my support to ubuntu.
I am not saying that I wasn't disappointed when I heard that the Ubuntu versions weren't cheaper in many cases, but the companies that sponsored my computer were the only losers in that deal because they don't get the exposure and support that they were looking for from me.  I'll admit though, that vista sticker will dig under your fingernails quite a few times before you can pry it off.

Feel free to pm,

Evan

### Post by markofealing on 2007-07-11
I've been buying Dells for nearly 10 years for work. The official line from Dell (according to my account manager at the time) when questioned over upgrade prices was "Dell charge a premium on upgrades to the base model for the consumer market".

Essentially their pricing strategy is to sell a bargain basement PC and then slap premium prices on the upgrades to make the profit. It's called a "loss leader" in marketing speak. The bottom line is if you are buying a Dell buy the specification as advertised and only upgrade where the prices are reasonable. The sticking point tends to be Hard Disks and Memory where you generally have to throw away the supplied hardware to upgrade with non Dell hardware at a cheaper price. This is where Dell gets you as generally it is not economic to take this approach so you are forced down their upgrade path.
[B]
This explains why in the original thread, there was such a big price difference between the standard Windows laptop price and the "upgraded" Ubuntu laptop. Using Dell prices, normalising the specification creates an artificial price difference. You would get a similar price difference if you were using just Windows PCs.[/B] 

It's a problem, but it is also Dell's business model for consumer PCs. Dell uses a volume pricing model for business customers and therefore hardware upgrade prices are reasonable.

I don't believe Dell are ripping off Ubuntu customers (it's not in their interest) no more than they are ripping off Windows customers. We should be grateful for Dell taking the bold step and backing Ubuntu Linux, it gives Ubuntu (and Linux) a fighting chance on the desktop.

---

-- If you're outraged, don't buy a Dell. If other people want to buy a Dell, leave them alone. Live and let live.

Did I miss anything?
Well you did miss one thing: now you have the option of removing that crapware before you buy it; really, see it for yourself in the "Add my software..." tab when you build a comp.  Under the different headings, there is always an option of having NO software of that type pre-installed.

Of course, there is still crapware installed by default, such as the infamous 30-day anti virus trial, and some other crap.  But still, can we still name this a reason for the windows dells to be cheaper than the ubuntu dells?

### Post by WebDrake on 2007-07-12
> **PrivateVoid said:**
> That IS not my point.

My points are:

1) Experienced Linux users will likely build their machine, but if they do buy a preconfigured machine they would likely not chose to buy an Ubuntu box and pay $200 for it.
2) Inexperienced users will likely shop based on price alone

Sure the Windows boxes might be subsidized (thought that is speculation unless a Dell rep is here to confirm it), but while that explains WHY the pricing is the way it is... it does not change the fact that the BEST value is to be had buying a Windows box with the same hardware as the Ubuntu box and then formatting the disk and installing Ubuntu.

I think we are all looking to see the same thing -- Linux/Ubuntu find some success in the marketplace.

Side A: is all excited that a Vendor is selling Linux and is ignoring that the way it is packaged will likely mean it won't get sold

Side B: is all excited that a Vendor is selling Linux and is upset that the way it is being packaged will likely mean it won't get sold

Side C: is running around screaming that this is all a nefarious plot by Microsoft in collusion with Dell to give Linux/Ubuntu a bad name

(for clarity I am one of the Side B people)

There may be 13 million reasons that things ended up the way they did for Dell... but that doesn't change the fact that if the Ubuntu boxes had been even to or cheaper than the Windows boxes that there is a chance that some "regular folk" would purchase one.

Its a step, but as much as it is exciting it seems to be full of legitimate issues.

I hope I have made myself more clear.

I was going to reply to your earlier post, but this one may be better.

On the specific point of price, the news is now suggesting this was a brief blip and any discrepancy will vanish, so that particular factor shouldn't be part of users' choice.

But more broadly than that, it seems to me that your real problem is that Dell's Ubuntu offering seems to be targeting *those who want it* rather than those who don't know about it---that they're primarily targeting the existing community rather than aiming to broaden the fold.  And contra to your perceived "side A" I don't *ignore* that but think it is a strategically correct thing for everyone concerned.

The reason is this: that when you are trying to introduce something to people, it's important that *they know what they're getting into*.  That's true of computers in general but particularly of Linux, because it has three key problems---it is not widely used, the software range is almost completely different from the other major platforms and it still has serious issues with hardware support.

The last problem is easy to see.  An inexperienced user who buys Linux on price rather than due to knowing the difference between this and Windows will not appreciate (or care) about the driver/firmware issues that we live with.  Presumably with Ubuntu preinstalled the computer itself will work without any issues but what happens when you want to buy a webcam or a printer or a scanner?  It may well not work, youhe so won't understand why and when you compare to your friend's Windows computer where these things can be plugged in largely without problems, you will come to the conclusion that Linux was cheaper than Windows because it's an inferior product.  And once alienated in this way, it's far harder to get this person back on side than it would be if they'd got Windows, learned about computers and come to Linux with knowledge and understanding of where they were going.

The software issue is similar.  Inexperienced computer users are unlikely to have proper understanding of why popular software products that "everyone" uses are unavailable or incompatible with Linux, or why not all media formats play or open effectively.  *Why* do my friend's Word documents not appear properly in OpenOffice? *Why* do some of my videos appear with horrible digital distortion? etc.  If people can't work or exchange data with their friends---most of whom will be using Windows---they will be no less disenchanted than with the hardware issue.  Unless you *know* that you are going to face these issues and make the conscious choice in advance that it's worth it, you will again consider Linux inferior.

(You will note that although I talk about "inexperienced computer users" many of the above problems are just as frustrating for people who know about *computers* but are novices as regards Linux---no less so even if they really want to learn about and use the new system.)

Now finally, the issue which I suspect on the surface sounds amazingly contradictory---that Dell shouldn't evangelise Linux because it's not widely used.  The issue is simply this---that for a novice, the best computing support they get is not from their supplier or from internet forums but from more experienced friends.  I've learned a lot from the forums here but what really made it possible for me to use Linux was having friends around me who already knew how.  We *don't* want to create a situation where novice computing users are popping up who don't understand how their machine works and can't call on their friends for help because their friends don't understand what to do either and just shake their heads and say, "You should have got Windows."

What we *do* want is a cost-effective supplier of reliable Linux machines so that as we---not Dell---spread the word and convert by example and demonstration, we have someone we can refer new users to for their hardware needs.  Someone who is there *when they want it*.

I think ultimately the widespread takeup of Linux, if and when it happens, is going to come about not through a price war but through the spread of knowledge and a shift in people's perceived interests.  The easy and well-priced availability of Linux machines is going to be necessary for the shift to happen but it is *not* going to be the cause.  We should see Dell's contribution not so much as publicity as putting in place the infrastructural groundwork for *our* evangelical efforts.

---

### Post by kurty_77 on 2007-07-12
In my dealing with dell I have found that one things is clear. EVERYTHING is negotiable!

I regularly check other countries websites/prices before I call Dell, whichever one has the best deal converted to CDN dollars is where I start my negotiations. 

So why no do the same thing with the linux boxes? Go online, build the windows box with all the discounts, subtract $50 US or so (what I have heard an OEM licenses of XP/Vista cost dell), and then phone up dell and tell them the only way you will buy the machine is with ubuntu preloaded at the price you have decided. repeat that for about 45 minutes and they will cave.

---

### Post by x86asm on 2007-07-12
> But more broadly than that, it seems to me that your real problem is that Dell's Ubuntu offering seems to be targeting those who want it rather than those who don't know about it---that they're primarily targeting the existing community rather than aiming to broaden the fold. And contra to your perceived "side A" I don't ignore that but think it is a strategically correct thing for everyone concerned.

That is an excellent point about the need to segment out the market to avoid a disaster::: sell Linux to someone who doesn't know what Linux is.

Though I'm still at a loss at how exactly Dell will net a profit on a Ubuntu system. The margins are thin, the base hardware is either sold near or below cost, and most of the profit stems from the partnership with Microsoft, crapware profits, system upgrades [a $10 dollar difference between a 80 and a 160 GB HD being sold at $80, a $30 dollar difference between 1  and 2 GB RAM being sold at $100, and so on], insurance/warranties, and other items. Yet they still can't catch a break; net profit margin for the company was at 4.5% last time I checked.

Sell the Ubuntu box $50 cheaper, and you have lost $50, plus another $30+ hit for no crapware, and gain $25-$50 for the Windows license.

Of course the above is just a dumb example...

We know Dell is manipulating the actual "loss" by comparing a "Ruby Red" [$20] Windows system to a "Jet Black" Ubuntu system, and I'm sure the cost difference of the Intel Wireless NIC is not the displayed $25 over the Dell branded NIC. Also, for all we know Dell still has to pay Microsoft for an OEM License regardless of what OS the system shipped with.

I'm also at a complete loss as to how Dell managed to publicize and sell Linux Desktop systems without Microsoft's permission or backlash? Have you seen the "Findings of Fact" in the last anti-trust case? Play ball and you reap all the benefits, and if you don't, good luck getting through the next week.

With a rumored 2% net margin on the avg system, at a price of lets say $700... Dell is not making anything here, perhaps even taking a hit.

Again, this is just a dumb example, and things are much more complex [costs involved for selling/providing Linux systems?], but considering the razor thin margins...

Everything I have mentioned tells me that either this was designed to fail from the start, and/or Dell is using the Ubuntu systems as a type of loss-leader that drives traffic to the site and sells a better, more profitable, Windows system.

Having said that, I think we are looking in the wrong direction. Dell's margins are the lowest in the industry. HP, on the other hand, might actually be able to profit from this.

We need to find an OEM that does not put out "Dude, you're getting a Dell!" type of commercials. Rather an OEM that caters to a different crowd that does not purely shop for the best price deal, but a quality product.

---

### Post by darkog on 2007-07-13
> Earlier this week, the DeveloperSide.NET Blog posted a reaction to a pricing error on the Inspiron 1420N configurator page. User the_watcher brought the discussion into IdeaStorm shortly after. We fixed the pricing error this morning. Erik_d, a member of our Consumer pricing team issued an update in the comment thread earlier today.

Here's what he said:

"As a member of Dell's consumer pricing team, I want to let you know that the recent pricing error on the Inspiron 1420 with Ubuntu was a simple oversight&#8212;the pricing promotions available on the Inspiron 1420 with Vista were not applied to the Ubuntu systems. But we have now fixed this error. If you check today, you should find the same promos in play on both the Ubuntu and the Vista systems. Thanks for calling this error to our attention&#8212;and keep the feedback coming."

 I'd like to reiterate Erik's sentiment: thanks to the blogging community for bringing the error to our attention, and sorry for any inconvenience this has caused.

it was a pricing error. it's over now. can we move on? jeezz louise. some people are high strung.

### Post by terrapisces on 2007-07-15
Do you really think these options are free? In a Microsoft world, most of the cost of a system is covered by what most would call, bloat-ware. You know all those little apps and freebie offers that show up on your fancy new Windows desktop... Now, have you ever seen one of those on a Ubuntu PC, be it a Dell or not?

**** Just incase I wasn't clear... When you buy an OEM (Dell,HP,Lenovo,etc) computer with Windows on it, you get all kinds of 'free' offers and ad-ware included from the factory. Companies pay the OEM to include these pieces of software, therefore subsidizing the cost of the system. In the linux world, there are no 'free' offers and such, so you are paying for the actual system. In the above mentioned case, the color panel is probably paid for by an AOL icon on the desktop. Does this make sense?*

### Post by Dead_$partan on 2007-07-16
> **technikalKP said:**

I don't think Dell are **selling** Ubuntu, any Dell product that has an n in the model name usually means its just the system without an OS or a free non supported OS like free DOS or possibly Ubuntu or other Linux distros.

Plus I think what you said above about dell ditching Ubuntu is bad, its a good thing that companies like Dell have decided to start shipping home systems with Linux, it shows that Linux is heading in the right direction and this in my opinion is a welcome move.  

If more companies start to do this then hopefully windows users wont be afraid to take the step of trying this free alternative to windows, it might help banish this perception people still have about Linux not being desktop ready/multimedia ready etc and it may also push developers, games makers etc to get their software running natively on Linux or at least have better support through other helper software like cedega and wine.

### Post by MoebusNet on 2007-07-25
Reference: Beating a Dead Horse

Am I the only one that flashed back to the movie "Pirates of Silicone Valley"? If memory serves (sometimes it doesn't) there was a comparison made between Bill Gates and David Rockefeller.

David Rockefeller was said to have made money from his competitors - he got the railroad companies that shipped his oil to sign a contract that would pay him  a rebate for every barrel of oil they shipped, whether his or somebody else's.

Bill Gates was claimed to have signed similar contracts with PC manufacturers; they paid him a rebate for every computer they shipped whether his operating system was installed or not.

If that kind of stuff is still going on, that would really tilt the playing filed in favor of MS, wouldn't you say?

---

### Post by starcannon on 2007-07-26
> **cleentrax said:**
> I got my facts right. Apparently it depends on which package you start with. There are three of the 1420s to customize. I started with the "middle" one because it was the best deal for the options I wanted. No such option exists for the 1420n

[[IMG]http://img509.imageshack.us/img509/9889/picture1dh6.th.jpg[/IMG]](http://img509.imageshack.us/my.php?image=picture1dh6.jpg)

This isn't price cheating, this is marketing. Business X has back orders on popular product Z, they have a butt load of comparable product Y, so they offer incentives to order Y which they can ship out the next day, they make Z less appealing financially, backorder crisis averted. 
There is no conspiracy here, its a simple matter of dealing with stock and getting the product out.
Good grief, can't please everyone, and some people can't be pleased at all.
I for one am happy to be able to buy a brand new tested and installed laptop with Linux on it.
YAY DELL! no more nightmares making something kind of work, this one just does.

### Post by aysiu on 2007-09-20
Of course other people saw this:
[Dell Ubuntu is it a good deal at all ??](http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=475813)
[Ubuntu & Vista Dells same price???!!!](http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=462784)

Actually, the Ubuntu Dell's are about $50 cheaper than the Vista equivalents. There was a brief time when the Vista ones got a $200 off promotion that the Ubuntu ones didn't, but it was an oversight that Dell corrected.

For more details read this:
[ Dell is cheating with Linux computer prices!!!](http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?p=3012985#post3012985)

**Edit**: As a matter of fact, I *just* went to the US Dell website and compared two laptops with the exact same specs (I had to change the Ubuntu laptop's hard drive from 80 GB to 120 GB to get them to match). The 1420N (the Ubuntu one) is $774, and the 1420 (Windows one) is $829. So it's a $55 difference instead of $50.

**Edit Again**: I've merged this one with the other three threads, as I foresee this is going to be a constantly recurring issue as long as Dell offers Ubuntu on the exact same models as it does for Vista.

### Post by backdoc on 2007-09-25
I thought about buying a new Dell with Ubuntu.  I went to the Dell home page and saw a couple of prominent links for laptops.  I clicked around and couldn't find any that offered Linux.  I was able to find them, but not through the prominent links.

The Vista laptop was $789 for a 120GB HD, 15" screen, 1.8gig proc, and 256 MB ATI graphics.  The processor is: AMD Turion&#8482; 64 X2 Dual-Core Mobile Technology TL-56.

The Ubuntu laptop was $749, but had a 80GB HD, 14" screen and an Intel video card (w/ and unspecified amount of memory).  The processor is: Intel® Core&#8482; 2 Duo T5250 (1.5GHz/667Mhz FSB/2MB cache)

$40 is nothing to pay for 40GB HD and a bigger screen.  And, to make matters worse, there is a big a$$ comment on the Ubuntu page that says, "Dell recommends Vista".  What the fsck?  I don't get it.  Are they selling Linux boxes or not?  Talk about half-a$$.

Needless to say, I was disappointed.  I'm glad to see Linux being offered as an option from major players.  But, this is only slightly better than not being offered at all.  

I think I'll not buy right now.  Or, maybe I'll check out HP's Linux offerings.

### Post by mountaingoat on 2008-05-19
I was shocked to see the price difference between a Dell Inspiron 1525 loaded with Ubuntu and another one with almost identical configuration loaded with Vista. The Vista version has a $359 coupon discount while the Linux version only offers $25.

I contacted Dell and their representative attributed the difference to the fact that the Windows version had better hardware and has more software loaded onto it so it give Dell more room for discounts. What?!? That makes no sense. I configured the linux version to match pretty much everything (but the wireless card) and ALL the software for Linux is FREE!!! What more discount do you want? This does not seem fair to the Linux customer.

This is the link to the starting page to set up the Vista version. 

[http://configure.us.dell.com/dellstore/config.aspx?c=us&cs=19&l=en&oc=DNDWPT1&s=dhs&dgc=CJ&cid=24471&lid=698541](http://configure.us.dell.com/dellstore/config.aspx?c=us&cs=19&l=en&oc=DNDWPT1&s=dhs&dgc=CJ&cid=24471&lid=698541)


And this is the starting page to set up the Linux version.

[http://configure.us.dell.com/dellstore/config.aspx?oc=dncwpl1&c=us&l=en&s=dhs&cs=19&kc=segtopic~linux_3x](http://configure.us.dell.com/dellstore/config.aspx?oc=dncwpl1&c=us&l=en&s=dhs&cs=19&kc=segtopic~linux_3x)


The only hardware config item I could not exactly match was the wireless card. The rest was of the configuration was identical.

- Intel Core 2 Duo T5550
- Glossy, widescreen 15.4 display
- Intel Graphics Media Accelerator X3100
- 3 GB Shared Dual Channel DDR2 at 667MHz
- 250 GB SATA Hard Drive (5400 RPM)
- CD/DVD Writer (DVD+/- RW Drive)
- Integrated 2.0 M Pixel Webcam
- 56Whr Lithium Ion Battery (6 cell)



This is the transcript of the conversation with the Dell representative.

1:10:45 PM      System 

You are now being connected to an agent. Thank you for using Dell Chat

That does not make any sense. 

 1:15:25 PM      BE_RR_Rep_Elli 

They're actually not the same starting configuration. The one with Vista starting price is $1008; it has a better processor, more memory, bigger hard drive, and stuff like the free trials of AOL, McAfee, etc.

 1:15:40 PM      BE_RR_Rep_Elli 

so there's more "room" to do discounting

 1:17:30 PM      Customer 

I configured them the same and the linux version was much more expensive. I chose the same processor, amount of memory, hard drive size, the same screen... I could not get the same wireless card but I doubt that makes up for the huge amount difference. I don't care about Vista that's why I want Linux. Actually the linux version should be cheaper due to the free nature of linux.

 1:17:45 PM      Customer

 1:18:34 PM      BE_RR_Rep_Elli

 1:20:00 PM      Customer

### Post by michaelaly on 2008-05-20
Proprietary software companies pay Dell and other manufacturers to include trial versions of their software, such as McAffee, Roxio, etc. The pricing difference may have something to do with deals struck with these or other bloatware companies. It likely has very little to do with Microsoft.

### Post by melrom on 2008-05-20
> **archer6 said:**
>  The problem I'm trying to avoid is wiping out the hidden recovery partition that will restore XP pro in the event a crash. Saves me from having to carry recovery disks which I do not have anyway. 


You won't wipe the utility partition. It is not hidden. Use gparted to partition the drives. The Dell Utility Partition comes up at ~100MB partition. Just don't touch it and it works fine. My XPS with XP Pro SP3 dual boots Ubuntu, and the Utility Partition is untouched and intact.

### Post by Twintop on 2008-05-25
I think another reason for this is that time has to be spent on QA and development to make sure that the hardware is going to work *before* the product ships. If things aren't working, the company will end up spending more in tech support costs than they would have if the system was working in the first place. Many, many more Windows machines are sold, so the cost per unit to cover the QA/Dev costs is lower than it is with Ubuntu. Add that to the fact that Windows licenses are cheap for OEMs and it isn't surprising that this is the case. If the OEMs had to pay full price for Windows, then we'd see a gap in Linux's favor.

---

### Post by macogw on 2008-05-27
It appears my reply went into the ether...

Dells are generally about $50 less with Ubuntu than with Windows.  Lenovos are $89 less.

When large companies charge more for Linux or no OS, it is because their contract with Microsoft both 1) requires that the OEM pay for a Windows license for every computer sold, regardless of whether it has Windows installed 2) enforces a penalty for each system sold without Windows.

In the case of small companies like System76 and ZaReason, you are paying about $100, maybe $200 more.  For that, you get better hardware, guaranteed Linux support, and in ZaReason's case, a wonderful open hardware warranty and all the customization you want.  You might think they're not really name-brand, but they're mostly built on Asus hardware, which is good quality.

In the case of companies like Emperor Linux, you are paying quite a lot because they take laptops bought at retail prices then preinstall Linux for you, and configure everything to work properly.  You get a big name brand and pre-installed Linux.

---

### Post by macogw on 2008-05-27
> **@_R_|\/|_@_G_E_|)_|)_0|\| said:**
> Priced Ubuntu?? Nuh-uh. That would ruin the whole concept of Ubuntu! The point of Ubuntu is to have a nice good OS with as much freedom! I say that if Ubuntu were priced at THIS point its selling rate would go down quite a bit.
The concept of Ubuntu is free as in speech, not necessarily as in beer.  Free Software is to protect the 4 Freedoms:
0. The freedom to run the program, for any purpose
1. The freedom to study how the program works, and adapt it to your needs
2. The freedom to redistribute copies so you can help your neighbor
3. The freedom to improve the program, and release your improvements to the public, so that the whole community benefits

Note that not one of them has a thing to do with price.

### Post by aysiu on 2008-05-27
What I've generally found is that Dell will normally price the Ubuntu model slightly cheaper than the Vista model, but every now and then, the Vista model will have a huge promotional offer (free RAM upgrade, free hard drive upgrade, etc.) that the Ubuntu one doesn't get, so it makes the Ubuntu one look more expensive.

If you look at what's going on on the Dell website, you can see it's not all about the cost of licenses or R&D. Why would a Vista laptop get a promotion like that and not the Ubuntu counterpart? They use the same hardware.

Why does the Ubuntu page start with *Not sure Open Source is for You?* with a note that *when you choose open source you don't get a Windows operating system*? Why does the customization page say *Dell recommends Windows Vista Home Premium* at the top in bold letters, even on the Ubuntu page?

I'd love to see the Windows laptops say *Not sure closed source is for you?* and *when you choose closed source you don't get a Ubuntu operating system*, and have a *Dell recommends Ubuntu* at the top of the Windows customization pages in bold letters.

Those pro-Windows bits on the Dell page indicate that something is fishy going on here. It can't be explained with simple economies of scale and such. It can be explained only through complicated deals with Microsoft of the "We'll give you a huge OEM discount on XP if you..." variety.

You cannot buy a PC with no OS from Dell or any other major manufacturer (Shuttle might be an exception).  They have all signed contracts with Microsoft saying that every computer they ship **must** include an OS.  The OS is usually FreeDOS, but the machine isn't truly OS-less.

### Post by sdennie on 2008-05-27
A lot of machines will include trial or time limited versions of software on it for Windows (a.k.a. Bloatware).  The software vendors pay Dell to include their bloatware and it drives down the cost of a Windows machine.  Logic dictates that a machine with a free OS would be cheaper than a machine with an expensive OS but, when you factor in the HUGE discount that MS likely gives Dell and the bloatware payoff from third party software companies, it may not actually be the case.

---

You might think you are sticking it to the man, but you are really helping line their pockets by purchasing Vista, even though, paradoxically, you may be saving yourself money.  Vote with your wallets!

### Post by RedRat on 2008-05-29
I think many of you are missing a point here as to cost. First off, MS probably "sells" XP or Vista to Dell for far less than $40, basically what it charges its own employees. But I suspect that the extra cost of the Ubuntu machines is that Dell maintains a separate Linux support group, that does not come free. This group is separate from Windows and from the comments I have heard from around the internet it is very good at dealing rapidly with problems. 

I bought a Dell 530n with Ubuntu installed and, through my own fault, had to install vanilla Ubuntu 7.10. The machine works very well. I have upgraded to Hardy via a download from the repo and Hardy with the addition now of the Nvidia-settings program now works like a charm. I am assuming that in order to get this compatibility with Linux, Dell has had to expend some money in engineering, this don't come free folks, there is no free lunch in this world. In addition, no question that volume is also involved, Dell has to maintain a Linux site and also purchase department, accountants want to cost out everything. So that they charge slightly more, is not surprising.

### Post by ugm6hr on 2008-05-29
> Is it fair for Laptops with Ubuntu to be priced higher than those with XP pre-installed?
Fair? No.

But vendors price according to market conditions.

Some countries have anti-competition laws which forbid price fixing etc.

Not sure about OEM suppliers, but educational institutions get reduced MS licensing fees if they pay for an MS license on **every** computer in the institution, irrespective of OS.  Apparently this does not breach "anti-competition" requirements (and these agreements have been encouraged by government agencies including in the UK).

### Post by frogitts on 2008-06-03
I agree with the whole trend of this discussion...I was so excited to hear that Dell was offering Ubuntu preinstalled, but there's no advantage to buying it. It kinda knocks the wind out of the open source movement in a way. We keep saying that the best part of Linux is that it's free, but with Dell, Linux costs money. With Dell, Windows is free, Linux is expensive. That makes no sense to me (even though I understand why it happens).

Take for instance, the Inspiron 1525. I customized them to get the exact same system, except for these differences, and was really surprised to see the difference in price:

System 1:
Ubuntu Linux version 7.10 with DVD Playback
Intel® 3945 802.11a/g Mini-card
Price: $649 ($599 after a $25 rebate)

System 2:
Genuine Windows Vista® SP1 Home Basic Edition
Dell Wireless 1395 802.11g Mini-Card
Price: $649

Apparently Windows costs $25. Somehow, I don't believe that, and what happens when they discontinue the huge $25 discount on Ubuntu systems? 

I thought the point behind Linux was to allow consumers to spend more on hardware. I would gladly pay the same amount for an Ubuntu system as I would for a Windows system as long as I got better hardware! Otherwise, what's the point? How is Ubuntu sustainable and meaningful if there's absolutely no economic advantage to anyone? I mean, other than the fact that we have an alternative if Windows develops artificial intelligence that takes over the world and the only way to save the day is by using Linux - which I do believe is an event we should prepare for :)

Linux versus Zombies!

But seriously, I'll keep using Ubuntu regardless because I agree with its ideals - I'm just worried about how this can continue.

### Post by unutbu on 2008-07-30
Maybe Dell is trying to price the 1525 (with Vista) to be competitive with other laptop manufacturer's offerings.

Also perhaps Dell regards its N-series line as competing in a different market than its mainline systems -- although this makes little sense to Linux users with a bit of knowledge.

Dell's Ubuntu line competes against System 76, which sometimes has higher prices for machines with similar specs. So with competitors' prices like that, perhaps Dell feels free to increase its price.

After reading the OP's post, I checked the price  of a basic Dell desktop (530 vs 530N), and here the 530N was $100 less than the 530. So for anyone interested in a Dell, it would behoove them to check both the main line and the 'N'-series price before buying.
You just can't tell which will be cheaper.

### Post by Talon2 on 2008-08-01

- Good deals are often applied to Vista systems but not Ubuntu systems.  You can usually correct this and get as good or a better deal by applying coupons such as those found here:

### Post by Temposs on 2008-08-01

Turns out if I make the lowest possible common system configuration for the 1525 and the 1525N at that time it was $5 cheaper for the Ubuntu system. So I think it depends on the upgrades you get and such.

I think a lot of the missing upgrades and components on the N series compared with the Vista series may be because they encounter some bug that makes that component not work properly in Ubuntu, so they pull it from their upgrade options until they can fix the bug, if they ever can. 

Similarly, I noticed that they pulled the entire 1420N off the site shortly before they upgraded to Hardy, and put it back two weeks later with Gutsy still being listed for it. So maybe they are having trouble upgrading that system to Hardy for some reason, eh?

I work as the primary "customer service" person in the admission office of a school. My boss makes all the decisions as to policies, but when families ask me questions about those policies, I'm able to explain the rationales informing those policies (even if I don't always personally agree with them) - I don't say "'Cause my boss says so. I have no idea why."

### Post by Hairyloon on 2008-08-19
I've been looking at the Dell Website, specifically the 1525 laptop.
I've discovered that you can get different deals for the same machine, depending upon which start point you customise from.
e.g. the basic model has a special offer: save £10 & free colour upgrade.
The next model up has a special offer save £50.
If you upgrade the basic one to match, then it comes out £40 more.

But then again, PC world undercuts them both.

Ignoring all that, I found much the same as OP; the Ubuntu version with the same hardware comes out the same price as Windows.

So I think I'll go to PC world & give them the software back for a refund, in accordance with the EULA.
[quote="Vista EULA"]By using the software, you accept these terms. [b]If you do not accept them, do not use the
software. Instead, return it to the retailer for a refund or credit. [/b]If you cannot obtain a refund
there, contact Microsoft or the Microsoft affiliate serving your country for information about Microsoft’s
refund policies.[/quote]

### Post by veritas366 on 2008-08-20
Microsoft is a rather formidable force in the marketplace and though I have no inside knowledge, I assume they aren't real happy with Dell's embrace of Ubuntu. So I'm sure they have to move carefully.

  If your motivation for buying Ubuntu is to save money then you will lose.  If you build up a baseline Vista system to the same specs as the Ubuntu, the Ubuntu system does come out a little less, but that's irrelevant.  There are usually dozens of specials available on the Vista systems...many of which are "loss leader" type..that is, the specials themselves don't make the company much profit but bring in customers who will hopefully buy other things as well.  

Also, I have to tell you that most sales reps know nothing about Ubuntu.  I just gave a little presentation to my own team about it, though I don't think they paid much attention (though the next day a rep got an Ubuntu call....maybe he'll pay more attention now.)  We get no training about Ubuntu and I even had another rep furiously trying to get my attention because he couldn't understand why he couldn't add anti-virus software to the Ubuntu machine!  I'm lobbying for them to put an Ubuntu system up in the demo lab so people can see how it all works.  So far, that hasn't happened.

I'll also be honest and tell you that the way reps are compensated, we don't have the possibility of getting much reward for selling Ubuntu systems.  There are simply very few upsell options.  I personally don't care about that one bit....and love it when I get Ubuntu calls.  But it's a high pressure climate for sales people there.  Please don't call in just to argue about pricing decisions for Ubuntu...it's totally out of our hands. (I know the above poster was using the chat feature which I assume has similar issues for those reps) Go to the Dell Idea Storm page and let them know how you feel.  If any of us sales people have to spend 20 minutes arguing about pricing with a customer....we suffer the consequences in compensation and performance evaluations.

They don't tell us ANYTHING about pricing decisions or why certain configurations are available one day but not the next.  We find out only by logging on and seeing what is available that day.  Give your sales reps a break.. if you can't find pricing that suits you, buy a Vista system or go elsewhere and then let Dell know via Idea Storm why you made your decision.  It's a legitimate issue but not one sales reps have any control over.

---

### Post by Loaded.len on 2008-08-23
Here's a little tip: Dell's return policy is 21 days (no questions asked).  That means that within the 21 day grace period from when you received your system you can tell them you don't like the shade of blue on the keyboard FN keys and they will accept it and refund your money. (in that scenario, you would simply take the money and re-invest it into the current offer) - since the 21 days are expired they can say no... HOWEVER. Dell is a business built on customer service. They can and will bend the rules if it means keeping you as a customer (within reason) I would suggest you keep trying and next time, ask to escalate it to a manager.

### Post by rafaelrc on 2008-08-24
My experience buying a Dell laptop with Ubuntu preinstalled was a pleasant one. I did manage to get a nice Inspiron 1525 for under 1000 dollars, (specs and prices below; they apply to Latin America). Having said that, I think I learned a few lessons along the way:

- Don't buy online. In my personal case, I compared the online specs and prices, then I called in. I could do a bit of "arm-wrestling" so to say with the sales representative. I told him I wanted a computer with this and that, but then he tried to make me buy some stuff I really didn't need (extended warranty, bluetooth). I could get at a point in which I could started to deal with the guy (I'll buy you the extended warranty, if you upgrade my original choice of CD burner with a DVD/CD player and burner with no extra cost). 

- This draws me to agree with a conclusion I read somewhere else: All Dell channels manage different prices: online, catalogs, phone representatives (Presumably, all Dell divisions compete with each other; hence the difference)... Try to make the most of them for comparing a Vista computer with an Ubuntu computer and try to be as informed as you can when you'll make the call.

- Don't fall for the first thing the salesperson offers you. Deal with him/her. Tell him or her you're on a budget, and that you will not spend more than X on a computer (set a reasonable amount from your conclusions from researching all channels).

- Rest assured that the salesperson will NOT offer you Ubuntu as first choice. Guys, we all love Ubuntu and free software as much as anyone. Heck, we wouldn't even be here if it wasn't for that. But in all honesty, we all know that we're not at a point in which Ubuntu has mainstream appeal. But we're all working on it, and personally I'm pleased to see Dell is bringing more models to its Ubuntu offering. If they could only match it with a bit more training to its sales personnel, just like Veritas pointed out.

The process for my purchase of a Dell laptop took a while because I took the time of doing all of that. And I don't regret it for a second. Hope this helps you.

This baby cost me 940 dollars at Dell via phone:

> ```

Inspiron 1525, Intel Core 2 Duo T7250, 2.0GHz, 800Mhz, 2M L2 Cache
Jet Black Color with Matte Finish
2GB, DDR2, 667MHz 2 Dimm
15.4 inch Wide Screen WXGA LCDTrueLife for Inspiron 1525
Intel Graphics Media Accelerator X3100
250G 5400RPM SATA Hard Drive
Ubuntu Linux version 7.10 withDVD Playback
Integrated 10/100 Network Card
8X DVD+/-RW Dual Layer Drive
Integrated High Definition Audio 2.0
Intel 3945 WLAN (802.11a/g) Mini Card
Integrated 2.0M Pixel Webcam
Software for Integrated 2.0M Pixel Webcam
56 WHr 6-cell Lithium Ion Primary Battery, for Inspiron 1525
Dell Hardware Warranty Plus Onsite Service Initial Year Basic Support: Next Business Day Parts and Labor Onsite Response Initial Year
NO WARRANTY YRS 2-3 (NBD) LA/BZ
CompleteCare Accidental Damage Protection, 1 Year 
```

### Post by aysiu on 2008-09-04
> **Nergar said:**

### Post by Miguel on 2008-09-04
OK, in Dell Spain, for normal notebooks offered with Ubuntu, the cheapest option is always linux, and it is 50$ less than the **equally** configured windows machine. However, they also have pre-configured machines with windows with higher specs. Trying to match those with the linux one, only available with the lowest config, ends up costing more. However, if you configure the middle-of-the-ground laptop like the high-end laptop (more ram, a more powerful CPU and a larger HD) it will also cost you more than selecting the high-end one directly. I suppose it's a similar deal for the mini 9.

As a side note, I'm considering buying either a Dell Latitude E6400 or a Thinkpad T400. If I get the Dell, I want the integrated GPU (less power, less weight than the nVidia plus free drivers) on almost the highest specced laptop. Well, it's almost more expensive to buy it without the nVidia graphic card! And let me tell you that the option, if available, costs 100€.

### Post by aysiu on 2008-09-04

That is, of course, the biggest problem, because people look at short-term benefits instead of long-term ones. That's why people buy from Wal-Mart instead of mom-and-pop stores. Wal-Mart is cheaper. Of course, those same people may criticize Wal-Mart for its business practices or for being a monopoly, but that's the price you pay.

You either pay the price with money (a little more to support "the little guy") or you pay with monopolies and unethical business practices.

I understand not everyone has a lot of money, but if you really believe in long-term choices for people, it's worth paying a little more to show that Linux is a viable option for OEMs to sell and make money off of. Maybe you can have a few less burger+fries+coke deals or you can save up for a few months longer in order to show your support for Ubuntu and your support for Dell providing a Ubuntu alternative.

It's not a lot more money to get the Linux option. It's a little more money. And that little more money also sends the message to Microsoft that they won't profit off your Linux use.

### Post by aysiu on 2008-09-04
We're also talking about a hypothetical "if you match the specs" price comparison, too.

Not everyone wants to match the specs, which is why I hate it when Mac fanboys bring up the whole "Mac isn't expensive... if you spec out the Dell laptop to match the same specs, Mac is cheaper or the same price" argument.

A lot of times, people don't want to match specs. Sometimes people actually want something cheap. No, I don't want a $2000 Windows laptop with the same specs as a $2000 Mac. I may actually want a $499 Windows laptop. Where's the $499 Mac laptop? Nowhere. Same deal with the Ubuntu Dell Mini 9. It's cheap, and it's got cheap specs. For people who use it as an internet appliance (its intended use), it's a good price.

I would argue it's mainly forum members who here like to spec-price-compare just so they have something to get outraged about.

### Post by ftolead on 2008-09-04
Yes, the Ubuntu configured one for $349 is "cheaper" than the Windows XP one next to it for $399. But if you click on the "Build Yours" button on each, you'll see that the Windows XP version comes with an 8gig solid state drive (4gig on Ubuntu version) and a .3 Megapixel built in Webcam (None on the Ubuntu version).

Click on the "Build Yours" under the $349 Ubuntu version, and substitute the 8 gig ssd($35) and the .3 MP Webcam ($15) and it equals the same price as the Windows XP version.

Fully load up the Ubuntu version with all options, except Alpine white color, brings the price to $494

Fully load up the "mid-range" Windows XP with all the options, except Alpine white, brings the price to $494. The mid-range version comes with a $40 "Instant Savings" discount.

Start with the "top-line" Windows XP version and load it up with all options, except white, brings the price to $479. The top-line version comes with a bigger ($55) "Instant Savings" discount

### Post by heiowge on 2008-09-07
I'm not sure if this has been fully answered yet or not, but I hear that the ammount of trialware you get on a PC / laptop affects the price.

Think of it this way - 

You can have a Linux powered PC that cost £300.  Thats it.

Or you can have the same PC with Windows for £400, but that machine's cost is offset by the £150 it gets paid by a bunch of companies to put trialware of Norton, or a million other bits of junk that you don't want and didn't ask for.

The linux machine can't run the trialware, so there's no payment from these companies.

So the windows machine costs £300 + £100 for windows - £150 revinue = £250

The linux machine costs £300.

The answer - buy the windows one and install linux.:)

### Post by aysiu on 2008-09-07
The Ubuntu one is not US$50 more than the Windows one.

If you max the specs, the Ubuntu one is US$15 more than the Windows one.

If you leave the specs as is, the Ubuntu one is $100 less than the most expensive Windows one.

The most expensive Windows version is US$449, has 1 GB of RAM, a 16 GB hard drive, and 0.3 megapixel webcam.

If you take the less expensive Windows version and upgrade it to 1 GB of RAM and a 16GB hard drive, it's US$464.

Same deal with the Ubuntu version (US$464).

It's a simple case of the most expensive base model having a better deal than configuring the least expensive base model.

Windows itself is "more expensive" than Windows if you match specs.

I've already [made the case that Ubuntu *should* cost more for Dell](http://www.psychocats.net/ubuntucat/dell-inspiron-mini-pricing-scandal/), so the fact that it costs only $15 more (and the same applies to the lower Windows model anyway) is a good thing.

And, if you're hard-up for money, the Ubuntu model is still the cheapest one.

---

### Post by Mr. Picklesworth on 2008-09-07
I don't know about you guys, but I would buy hardware with Linux pre-installed because that ensures I will be able to run a Linux-based operating system with 100% complete hardware support out of the box. Tinkering to find hardware that supports Linux costs a lot of time, and similarly, having the manufacturer buy and (at times) tweak software for Linux-friendly hardware is well worth the extra price.

In Dell's case, it also costs money to license restricted codecs and DVD support so that they can distribute the computers in the US.

### Post by mikewhatever on 2008-09-14
> **vectadata said:**
> Hi everyone. I just ordered this awesome little netbook last night, but I was very disappointed to see a later ship date AND additional costs for the Ubuntu version. So I went ahead and ordered the CHEAPER Windows version. However, I am going to put Linux on it and email Dell informing them that I only bought the Windows XP version because it was cheaper. 

I wanted:

Obsidian Black
1gb RAM
16gb SSD
No webcam/bluetooth

If you want Ubuntu on that netbook, it may be wiser to reconsider the order. 
Firstly, the netbook version of Ubuntu is not yet available for download. 
Secondly, you don't know how hard (or easy) it will be to install the netbook, or the regular version.
Thirdly, the hardware for XP and Ubuntu may not be the same. 
I am just speculating here, but Dell sometimes uses Broadcom wireless cards for Inspiron 1525 with Windows and Intel 3945 with Ubuntu.
Anyway, whatever you decide, good luck.

Update: #1 has become irrelevant, since UbuntuRemix has been released.

### Post by starcannon on 2008-09-14
When I preordered my mini 9 a couple days ago the Windows machine was more expensive of equal specs, but had an instant rebate(presumably from MS) making it exactly the same price. 

MS underestimated the UMPC market, and is now scrambling with their outdated OS to catch up. This could be good news for XP users who for good reasons do not want to upgrade to Vista, as it could offer up the benefit of an extended period of support; however, if MS decides to drop support for XP on schedule, then it could mean people buying UMPC's with MS Windows XP on them, will find themselves on their own in a very short amount of time comparatively speaking.

Best bet, stick with the Linux OEM installs, regardless of political views on operating systems, at the end of the day, Linux makes UMPC's sing. Everything that would be reasonable to expect from a UMPC is handled incredibely well by Linux, I have four asus eee's and have installed Ubuntu on 3 of them, one of them is a 2g and I used Puppeee linux on it. All of them have exceeded expectation. 

GL and have fun

### Post by JEJ514 on 2008-09-17
I ordered a Mini 9 last week (with XP, but plan to add Ubuntu & double boot).  Here is my guess as to what is going on:

- The Ubuntu version isn't actually available yet.  They're taking orders for it, but the lead time for the Ubuntu version is 5 weeks vs. 2 weeks for the XP version (even if spec'd identically.)  No reason given for this, just a fact.

- The two XP versions that ARE released now have discounts on them.  They want to move what they have.  I wouldn't be surprised if they offered a discount (and possibly more color options?) when the Ubuntu one is actually ready to sell.  It is these discounts that lower the price of the XP ones below Ubuntu.

- If you consider it a trustworthy source, twice I confirmed with Dell's customer service folks that the ISO image of the Ubuntu install will be available for download from Dell's website.  Do with that what you will.  I'm hoping that is the case and that everything works.  I'm also confident in the Ubuntu community and expect these puppies to have decent community support (although perhaps not as fanatical as the EEE's support.)

Jeff
