### Post by Papa-san on 2008-10-07
> **stchman said:**
> Hello all,

I just went to Dell's website and priced out the 1525 vs the 1525N.  I equipped them as identical as possible.  The 1525 has a 4965 card (works with Hardy) and the 1525N has a 3945 card.

I know that a Windows machine has a lot of bloatware but not $249 worth.

What gives?

I guess just buy the Vista laptop and install Ubuntu.
I bought what was supposed to be three identical systems with the Vista Virus pre-installed. The systems arrived with different screens. (2 glossy, 1 anti-glare) and different wireless adapters. (2 4965's and 1 3945)

I installed 8.04 on two of them (Dual boot with Vista). The one with the 4965 never managed to function wirelessly, while the Intel 3945 (the cheaper card) worked out-of-the-box. 
I ended up receiving a request from my daughter (who's actually my Linux fan) to remove the Ubuntu, as it didn't meet her needs. I did this, and she's been happy with the Vista system. :-( 

On my wifes system, the Ubuntu ran splendidly for a long time. Never had any kind of issues with it. My wife (who's been a really hard sell on Ubuntu) was quite pleased for all this time. Eventually, I updated it, and it wrecked it. My wireless has to be manually re-configured and started with each re-boot. I experience several crashes each day, and am required to do a hard re-boot. The OS runs pretty slow on a relatively quick system. (Using Vista, I get my stuff done in about half the time it takes to do those same things in Ubuntu.) I've started a few threads on the problems, but have basically been posting to myself in them. 

What I find to be frustrating is that I cannot get any Ubuntu support from Dell, as I didn't purchase them with it pre-installed. I guess this is probably not a problem for hard-core *nixers, but for those of us who just want a good, safe, stable system instead of the Windows boxes we've always used, it can be way too much... (My wife has stopped trying to boot into the Ubuntu partition anymore, and will likely not return until I fix the issues.)

Just figured I would pass this along for anyone else who might be contemplating the purchase of a Vista system to additionally install Ubuntu.: If you aren't a whiz with tweaking it, you might be better off to get the 1525n...

Oh... BTW, my understanding of the price difference is that Microsoft gives Dell money (Massively discounts their license fee, actually) to offset certain costs. This is why there is a price difference... Dell doesn't actually have to pay Microsoft much at all to install their product...

### Post by cisk4me on 2008-12-31
Yesterday I compared the Inspiron 1525 with Ubuntu and Windows on the Dell UK web site and found a price difference of nearly GBP150 for my configuration (GBP 875 and 726).
The common parts are below
```
	Base:
Intel® Core&#8482; 2 Duo Processor T8300 (2.40 GHz, 800 MHz FSB, 3 MB L2 cache)
	Memory:
4096MB 667MHz Dual Channel DDR2 SDRAM [2x2048]
	Hard Drive:
320GB (5.400rpm) SATA Hard Drive
	LCD:
15.4" Wide Screen WXGA+ (1440 x 900) Display with TrueLife
	Camera:
2.0 Mega pixel web camera
	Video Card:
Integrated Intel Graphic Media Accelerator X3100
	Primary Battery:
9-cell 85WHr Li-Ion primary battery
	Colour Choice:
Ruby Red Colour with Microsatin Finish
	Power Supply:
65W AC Adapter
	Cables:
Power Cord UK 1meter
	Bluetooth:
Dell Wireless 355 Bluetooth 2.0 Module (up to 3Mbps) with Enhanced Data rate - Eur
```

The different parts are below, first Windows and then Ubuntu.

The Windows option  Price GBP 726.53
```
	Optical Devices:
DVD +/- RW Drive (read/write CD & DVD)
	Wireless Networking:
Dell Wireless 1395 802.11b/g Mini-Card - Europe
	Modem:
56.6k V.92 Capable Internal Modem & Adapter - UK
	Service:
1Yr In-Home Warranty Support, including evenings and Saturdays
	Microsoft Operating System:
Genuine Windows Vista® Home Premium SP1 - English
	Dell System Media Kit:
Resource DVD Inspiron 1525 - (Diagnostics & Drivers)
```

The Ubuntu option  Price GBP 875.52
```
	Optical Devices:
8x DVD+/-RW Optical drive - N-Series
	Wireless Networking:
Intel Pro Wireless 3945 802.11a/b/g Mini-Card - Europe - Core 2 Duo Processors
	Modem:
Dell v92 Data/Fax Modem - UK
	Enhanced Service Packs:
1 Year Limited Warranty - Collect & Return
	OS-Linux:
Ubuntu Edition version 8.04
	Dell System Media Kit:
No Resource CD
```

   I don't know whether the DVD, wireless and modem supplied with Windows are better or worse than the Ubuntu ones. But I do know that any difference in value is not GBP150. The Windows warranty is better than the Ubuntu one and worth GBP40 in configurations where it can be removed.

   I hear the excuse that Windows can be given away for free because its cost is paid for by the installed crapware. For this to be true, you would have to believe that the entire OEM operating system business of MS, the largest software manufacturer in the world, could be competley subsidised by part of the advertising budget of the crapware installers. It's just not credible.

   Instead it's more likely that Dell is choosing to cripple its own Linux business.

Whilst the software is free of charge, the choice of pre-installation isn't.

### Post by aysiu on 2009-04-21
As you can see, from the thread I merged yours with, this is an ongoing problem with Dell.

Theoretically (as seen through press releases and Dell staff responses on Dell's IdeaStorm), Dell is supposed to price Ubuntu cheaper.

What happens, though, is every now and then there are promotional offers (free RAM upgrade, free extra battery, an extra $200 off) for Vista computers that Dell "forgets" to also apply to the Ubuntu ones. If you badger Dell enough, they'll "remember" to apply it to Ubuntu as well... until the next promotional offer.

Then the cycle will repeat.

**More details**:

From *[Dell fixes Ubuntu laptop pricing](http://www.desktoplinux.com/news/NS9933912441.html)* (12 July, 2007)... > "Bottom line this was an oversight, pure and simple," a Dell spokesperson told DesktopLinux.com. "We will be posting a comment to IdeaStorm to that effect by tomorrow." In the meantime, Dell says that the prices have been reset to the appropriate prices.

[Same price for Windows and Ubuntu !](http://www.ideastorm.com/ideaView?id=087700000000FauAAE)
[Implement a system by which Ubuntu systems automatically get promotional discounts their Windows counterparts get](http://www.ideastorm.com/ideaView?id=087700000000C1DAAU)
[Mini 9 netbook Ubuntu price must be cheaper than XP price with same config](http://www.ideastorm.com/ideaView?id=087700000000BpcAAE)
[Make ubuntu version of laptop cheaper than Vista version](http://www.ideastorm.com/ideaView?id=087700000000BdNAAU)
[Please make Ubuntu XPS Notebook cheaper than XPS Vista Notebook](http://www.ideastorm.com/ideaView?id=087700000000BJgAAM)
[Stop Ripping us off: make Linux systems cheaper than equivalent Windows](http://www.ideastorm.com/ideaView?id=087700000000B1JAAU)
[Explain why you made Windows cheaper than Ubuntu again for the same hardware](http://www.ideastorm.com/ideaView?id=0877000000009coAAA)

This one's the most hilarious... (24 May, 2007)

[Implemented: Ubuntu Dell is Le$$ Than Windows Dell](http://www.ideastorm.com/ideaView?id=0877000000008ncAAA) [quote=dell admin]Changed status to **IMPLEMENTED*[/quote][quote=dell admin]On average, comparably configured Ubuntu systems will be about $50 less than Windows systems.[/quote]

### Post by RedRat on 2009-08-11
I believe, and I have only vague hints that his is the case, but I think MS charges the manufactures something like $35-$45 per Windows install. The price obviously depends on volume, etc.For all I know, for a large manufacturer like Dell or HP, the price may even be lower. So I don't think the price of Windows is doing much for the price difference. 

Since I haven't checked lately, but I think the slightly higher price may include costs of keeping the Linux support going (BTW, Ubuntu support at Dell is reached by a separate phone number than normal Dell support, normal Dell support may not even know about Linux). Dell must have some kind of team that ensures that Ubuntu works on their various machines and since volume sales are probably low, I imagine that this makes Ubuntu Dells more expensive. Just part of the overhead for Linux at Dell. Don't think it is all that unreasonable.

In the end, you could just buy the Windows version, wipe the hard disk and install Ubuntu.

### Post by mikewhatever on 2009-08-12
> **RedRat said:**
> I believe, and I have only vague hints that his is the case, but I think MS charges the manufactures something like $35-$45 per Windows install. I don't like discussing estimates when there is no way to verify them, so let us stick to the facts. Windows licenses cost money to OEMs, Ubuntu is free, and I expect that difference to show, however large or small it might be.

> Since I haven't checked lately, but I think the slightly higher price may include costs of keeping the Linux support going (BTW, Ubuntu support at Dell is reached by a separate phone number than normal Dell support, normal Dell support may not even know about Linux). Just part of the overhead for Linux at Dell. Don't think it is all that unreasonable.

[http://www.psychocats.net/ubuntucat/inspiron-15n-dell-finally-properly-prices-its-ubuntu-option/](http://www.psychocats.net/ubuntucat/inspiron-15n-dell-finally-properly-prices-its-ubuntu-option/)

IMO, that solution is completely unacceptable because of the message it sends to OEMs, which is, There is no market for OEM Linux. There is no way OEMs will keep offering Linux preinstalled if Linux users buy computers with Windows lightly hopping over a Linux offer.
I am not even talking loosing the warranty by that action.

### Post by misGnomer on 2009-08-12
> **mikewhatever said:**
> Windows licenses cost money to OEMs, Ubuntu is free, and I expect that difference to show, however large or small it might be.

It's most certainly the case that there is a team of engineers working to provide compatibility for Ubuntu, however, it doesn't justify lying. Dell has promised time and again to price Ubuntu offers fairly, and failed to do so.

I suppose *the* permanent solution to this problem would be for Dell et al to offer their software offerings separately with clear and consistent pricing for the options Linux / MS-Windows / No OS.

It's not like Dell can't do it, since they already have options for "upgrading" from one MS-Windows version to another.

Europe should kick that ball rolling downhill by demanding that *optional* sales items are clearly optional and separately priced from the actual sales item (aka hardware).

I shouldn't have to enter into a compulsory add-on deal requiring me to prepay for several years' worth of gas from Shell when I go shopping for a new VW Golf...

> > **RedRat said:**
> In the end, you could just buy the Windows version, wipe the hard disk and install Ubuntu. IMO, that solution is completely unacceptable because of the message it sends to OEMs, which is, There is no market for OEM Linux.

Exactly. The main reason why "the market" perceives there to be little demand for Linux and Linux-based development is that the vast majority of Linux installs have been counted as MS-Windows sales!

Anyway, in another thread I noted that [the newly introduced Vostro 1014]("http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=1236404") was significantly cheaper with Ubuntu preloaded (US$398 in Hong Kong, with the current ~US$65 online discount) than the similarly equipped MS-Vista-encumbered version in Singapore (US$553).

Even without the online discount the HKG offering is still ~US$90 cheaper than the SIN version. This comparison is of course apples-vs-oranges and in the US the coupon specials invariably favour MS-ware so getting software add-ons priced separately is really the ultimate solution.

### Post by HotShotDJ on 2009-08-12
> **mikewhatever said:**
> I don't like discussing estimates when there is no way to verify them, so let us stick to the facts. Windows licenses cost money to OEMs, Ubuntu is free, and I expect that difference to show, however large or small it might be.The situation is a bit more complex than the seemingly simple math of subtracting out the cost that an OEM pays to MS for the right to install Windows.  There is also the money paid TO large OEM's when trial software is installed on computers.  For example, for each laptop that Dell installs Norton AV trial version (or whatever brand they are currently installing), Norton pays Dell some amount of money -- just like paying to advertise on TV, radio, bill boards, etc.  Again, just like Windows licenses, we don't know how much money that is, but we know it is a significant source of income for Dell, which is why they resisted selling "crap-free" computers for so long.  I believe there is still trial software that they will not let customers opt-out of (although it is much better than before).  Each time a customer purchases a Dell computer with Linux, Dell loses the advertising revenue for that computer.

As far as I can tell, Dell believes that it is in its best interest to keep their Linux sales as low as possible -- 1) it will keep the Microsoft hammer from falling on their head, and 2) it will keep advertising (crap-ware) revenues flowing in.  (Having said that, I hope that people in the market for a low-end computer with Linux continue buying from Dell.  My E1505 has been very satisfactory)

---

### Post by RedRat on 2009-08-12
> **HotShotDJ said:**
> ...

As far as I can tell, Dell believes that it is in its best interest to keep their Linux sales as low as possible -- 1) it will keep the Microsoft hammer from falling on their head, and 2) it will keep advertising (crap-ware) revenues flowing in.  (Having said that, I hope that people in the market for a low-end computer with Linux continue buying from Dell.  My E1505 has been very satisfactory)

My innate cynicism kicks in and tells me you may well be right. Selling Linux computers does reduce revenue flow for each computer/unit sold. After all, Dell is a business and must make money to reward stockholders and others at the company. At any given time, of course, Dell could refuse to have any OS installed and basically sell virgin computers, basically a box. They obviously don't do this. 

Of course because of the low stream of Linux sales at Dell, I would imagine that putting Linux on the machines and then offering support for the computer afterwards raises the cost per unit due to low sales numbers. It is the old story of how you make a good profit: volume, volume, volume. While many in the Linux community see Ubuntu and other variants as free, there is no free lunch with any OS. Somewhere, sometime, someplace someone will need help and advice. While advice may be "free" it does cost. Not all Dell/Ubuntu problems can be resolved here in the forum. Maintaining a support phone number with paid staff does cost Dell money and someone has to pay, that is the customer.

### Post by RedRat on 2009-08-12
> **mikewhatever said:**
> 
IMO, that solution is completely unacceptable because of the message it sends to OEMs, which is, There is no market for OEM Linux. There is no way OEMs will keep offering Linux preinstalled if Linux users buy computers with Windows lightly hopping over a Linux offer.
I am not even talking loosing the warranty by that action.

From a ideological purity point of view, you may have a legitimate point. However, I don't find myself in that hallowed space. Clearly, the real everyday market for Linux based machines is low compared to Windows machines. Most buyers demand Windows and only want to use Windows. That is a sorry fact of life. Microsoft still makes money from Dell and any other manufacturer who sells Windows based computers. MS knows full well that Linux and its variety of distros are all out there, free for the taking. IMO, buying a Windows machine because the price is right and wiping it of Windows and putting Linux on the machine, has little or no effect on any manufacturer who might be willing to offer Ubuntu or other Linux distro. 

The real question is one of incentive to sell Linux machines. What exactly is it? As we have discussed above, Dell makes money on putting crapware on their machines. Norton and others pay them to do this. This makes Windows machines a profit center, money from crapware and the profit on building and selling the machines. Linux machines on the other hand, using this simplistic accounting approach, really represents a cost center. Yes the OS is free, but engineering the machines so that Linux will run does cost, and maintaining a support line also costs money that Dell must recoup. 

Frankly, I am surprised that Dell even offers to put Linux on their machines and offer even a modicum of support. Because they do this, I am a bit more willing to consider Dell when I purchase my next machine.

---

### Post by mikewhatever on 2009-08-13
> **RedRat said:**
> I would not be so harsh toward Dell in accusing them lying. After all Dell said that it would price them fairly, and the definition of what is fair is then in Dell's ball court, not yours. What you or I may think is a fair price for a machine is our own individual evaluation. 
.......

I haven't decided anything about Dell's pricing, Dell has. You should look at the link I posted again.

> **RedRat said:**
> From a ideological purity point of view, you may have a legitimate point. However, I don't find myself in that hallowed space. Clearly, the real everyday market for Linux based machines is low compared to Windows machines. Most buyers demand Windows and only want to use Windows. That is a sorry fact of life. Microsoft still makes money from Dell and any other manufacturer who sells Windows based computers. MS knows full well that Linux and its variety of distros are all out there, free for the taking. IMO, buying a Windows machine because the price is right and wiping it of Windows and putting Linux on the machine, has little or no effect on any manufacturer who might be willing to offer Ubuntu or other Linux distro. 

Call it as you wish, ideology, hallowed space, the fact remains that if Linux users buy Linux based machines, OEMs will keep selling them, or at 
least offer an option sans OS. Buying Linux preinstalled, or buying computers sans Windows, will tell OEMs there is a market and profit to be made on hardware alone.
I'd like to share a bit of personal experience to that end. Dell doesn't maintain a store where I live and operates through resellers. In 2009, some of the resellers started offering most modells without Windows preinstalled. About a month ago, I called one of the resellers and ordered a Dell mini 10, casually mentioning I didn't want it with Windows XP (there wasn't a sans OS offer). The salesman asked why, to which I said that I already had an OS. Then he hesitantly mentioned that the XP home license for the netbook only costs 32$, and I readily suggested it to be discounted from the total price in local currency. The salesman told me to wait, and two minutes later was back telling I could have it my way. The price got from good to better, I didn't have to buy Windows, and Dell didn't loose a penny.


> The real question is one of incentive to sell Linux machines. What exactly is it? As we have discussed above, Dell makes money on putting crapware on their machines. Norton and others pay them to do this. This makes Windows machines a profit center, money from crapware and the profit on building and selling the machines. Linux machines on the other hand, using this simplistic accounting approach, really represents a cost center. Yes the OS is free, but engineering the machines so that Linux will run does cost, and maintaining a support line also costs money that Dell must recoup. 

Frankly, I am surprised that Dell even offers to put Linux on their machines and offer even a modicum of support. Because they do this, I am a bit more willing to consider Dell when I purchase my next machine.

As said before, I don't like meaningless discussions based on speculations. So what if Dell and others sell crapware. We don't know and have no way of finding out how much profit that business generates. Dell could be getting a dollar fifty for a copy of Norton preinstalled, or none, unless a customer upgrades through Dell. How does that translate to > 50$ discounts? After all, if crapware covers all the costs, Dell could just have becme Norton's receller and dump its main business. 

What we know for sure is that Dell is an OEM, selling hardware and support. Not having to pay for an operating system for that hardware is a major saving factor regardless of crapware and MS discounts to OEMs. Yes, Dell needs a team of engineers to make sure Ubuntu works on the selected models, and a line of support, but the same goes with Windows, and they also have to support ten times more models and probably a thousand times more customers, all of which does cost and must be recouped.

For someone who doesn't like to speculate, your reply seems filled with them. It is not speculation that Dell clearly makes some money on crapware pre-installed. If they did not, then they wouldn't do it. That is economics 101. Why would they do that if there were no money to be made. In the very early days, Dell sold computers without any OS, just the machine. In fact my first Dell came without a pre-installed OS. My understanding was that when an OEM cuts a contract with MS, MS used to collect a "Windows Tax" on each computer that the OEM sold. Now whether that is still true or not, I don't know. But I would "speculate" that it may not hold true anymore, that may well be why Dell now sells Linux machines. 

As to your anecdotal story about the netbook, you are really speculating, just like me, that the $32 was taken off the price because you didn't buy the XP OS. Perhaps, the salesman, just discounted the asking price by $32. Or he was hoping that you would kick in an extra $32 for the license. After all, there is a profit margin built into each machine sold. Better to sell at $32 less than have no sale at all. 

All of the discussions we are having are all speculation, since neither one of us sits in the Dell boardroom. We don't know what the pricing strategy of Dell is, we know only what we see that comes out of Dell. 

You say:
"What we know for sure is that Dell is an OEM, selling hardware and support. Not having to pay for an operating system for that hardware is a major saving factor regardless of crapware and MS discounts to OEMs. Yes, Dell needs a team of engineers to make sure Ubuntu works on the selected models, and a line of support, but the same goes with Windows, and they also have to support ten times more models and probably a thousand times more customers, all of which does cost and must be recouped."

 This is pure speculation on your part because neither you nor I really know the cost figures for each Dell line. Running the Windows support center costs money, how much we do not know. But Dell must also run a support center for Ubuntu, perhaps not as large as the Windows center, but it does cost. The sale of Linux machines we pretty much know is much smaller than the Windows equivalent line and we all know that volume reduces costs, that is a business fact of life. Debating how much that cost is, of course, pure speculation. 


Any OEM in the business is really there first and foremost to sell hardware, Microsoft is in the business to sell software. An OEM will put anything on their machines that will help and aid in selling their machines, whether that be Windows, MacOS, or Linux. They will put crapware on it if it helps to sell the machine and add to their line's profit margin. In the end, any OEM will put whatever OS on the machine that the customer asks for if it sells the machine. For Dell and the others, Windows helps to sell machines and added hardware. 

Why Dell chose to add Linux to their machines, I don't know. I am assuming that a sufficient number of customers requested it, perhaps the addition of Ubuntu was a spill over from their business sales of Red Hat or other UNIX systems. But in the end, there must be an incentive to offer these machines. Once that incentive goes away, so do the machines sold with Linux.


So as to fairness of the price, I only look at the machine I buy and judge the price to be fair or not, I particularly don't care if the same model has Windows on it and sells for more or less. The only question I ask is "Will this machine do (fill in the blank) and how much do I have to pay?" As I said, I have built computers from scratch, and I do know the cost of parts. I still think that Dell's prices are quite fair for their Linux machines.

---

### Post by HotShotDJ on 2009-08-13
> **mikewhatever said:**
> As said before, I don't like meaningless discussions based on speculations. So what if Dell and others sell crapware. We don't know and have no way of finding out how much profit that business generates. Dell could be getting a dollar fifty for a copy of Norton preinstalled, or none, unless a customer upgrades through Dell. How does that translate to > 50$ discounts? After all, if crapware covers all the costs, Dell could just have becme Norton's receller and dump its main business.Please see [http://arstechnica.com/old/content/2007/01/8598.ars](http://arstechnica.com/old/content/2007/01/8598.ars)

I was not speculating. Dell has admitted that it receives money from software vendors for installing trial software, and that the amount of money is non-trivial.  We also know that Dell stated (in 2007) that a $60 premium would be sufficient to cover the cost of selling a unit without crapware.

I, too, have an anecdote regarding Windows.  My Dell E1505 was supposed to come with Windows XP (which I planned on removing, installing Ubuntu, and reinstalling XP in a virtual machine).  Unfortunately, it was shipped with a "Free Vista Upgrade."  No XP to be found.  When I called to complain, they said there was nothing they could do -- I was stuck with Vista unless I wanted to return the laptop.  I escalated my complaint, and was offered a $160 credit, which I accepted and just a few days later, saw a $160 credit on my credit card.  I think we can all agree that this amount does NOT reflect the cost of a Vista Home Premium OEM license (in fact the Dell associate who gave me the credit was very careful to tell me that this was not a "Windows Refund" and that I was still free to reinstall Vista in the future, if I changed my mind).  Keep in mind, I had already taken advantage of a $200 employee/student discount on the machine.

I don't know how much crapware was installed on my computer, since I never ran it with the installed OS -- I got as far as the Windows Vista license agreement screen, from which the only "exit" was a hard shutdown using the power button -- but whatever it was, it was worth at least $160 to Dell.

In the end, I'm finding that Dell excels in cheap, mass-produced, low-end computers. I don't say that derisively. I needed exactly that 3 years ago, and I looked to Dell.  Now that I'm in the market for a higher end system, I'm looking elsewhere and finding some excellent hardware and prices that Dell simply can't match.  And I won't have to have software that I don't want forced down my throat.

As I said before, my labor may not be worth $50-$100/hr, but it sure as heck isn't $0/hr.

Look, Dell may not have the highest performance machines around (either for Windows or Linux), I think they pretty much fall somewhere in the middle ground. I think the offer reasonable value for the money.

There's no office software, no decent video editing software, no decent image editing software, no Adobe Acrobat, no PDF creation software, no nothing? Perhaps it would be better said that Windows will not be ready for the general public until it is impossible to make a profit from a business like GeekSquad.

And what's this about not needing help forums if Linux were good enough for the general public?  Thats just not true.  Windows users need just as much help (if not more) than Linux users... GeekSquad, Help Desks, 24/7 1-800 numbers, etc.  My university has an insanely large budget just for the IT help desk for faculty and students -- and they ONLY support Windows XP. You too easily dismiss things that you do not like to hear by saying it is irrelevant. I certainly beg to differ. I have installed Windows on a number of machines and installation is pretty straight forward. I have also installed Linux on several computers and it is not quite so straight forward as you imply. Just go to the Beginners Forum here and see the problems. As to mulit-meida and graphics, I would say that is at the core of most of the complaints with Ubuntu. Dismissing such difficulties as "unusual" or isolated" begs the question. I have yet to experience graphics problems on the dozen or so Windows machines that I installed, even after changing the video card and the monitor! That is certainly not the case in Ubuntu. Again, go check the beginners forums and general forums here. I am not saying that Windows is completely without problems, it has its shares, but installation is not really one of them. The problems with Windows and Macs have nothing to do with the installation processes but with all of the other stuff in the OS once you get going.

As to pre-installed software, Office and PDF capabilities, these may or may not be problems and their resolution is just as trivial as it is in Ubuntu--go download OpenOffice or Adobe Acrobat. In fact, some packaged computers offer MS Office and Adobe Acrobat as part of a bundle. 

As to your other comment about "decent" software, again that is in eye of the beholder isn't it? What exactly do you mean by "decent". For many Movie Editor in Windows is sufficient, and it is for relatively simple editing. I have complained in the multimedia section about the movie editing software for Ubuntu in that most of that software cannot handle the importation of HD-video from the current crop of HD cameras. I plaintively asked if anyone knew of software that could handle editing HD video and got no responses. I believe now that Lives may do this, I have yet to try it. Again, what is decent for you is not decent for me. 

The problem is that far too many snarky geeks live in a "fan-boy" dreamworld, basically it is "I'm all right Jack", "I've got my installation going and if you can't get your going you are somehow deficient". 

All developers, no matter what the project, have to sit down and evaluate just who are going to be their customers and/or end-users. Microsoft and Apple certainly do this and do it very well. The Ubuntu developers, I think have at least made some kind of attempt at doing this, but this is very hard and expensive thing to do. MS and Apple have enormous financial resources at their disposal and spend quite literally tens of millions, if not hundreds of millions, on this activity. Linux and Ubuntu were initially developed by UNIX geeks for UNIX geeks, so this is part of the Ubuntu DNA and it will be difficult to get the current coterie of developers to move to another mind-set. But if Ubuntu is to move beyond being a third tier OS, a plaything for geeks, and try to impact the main-stream Linux developers must start to design for others than their fellow geeks.

Oh, and about your 70 year old grandmother, hate to rain on your parade but you are discussing this with me, a 70 year old grandfather! As to my comments about the reduction of the Ubuntu forum, lighten up, did you not see my smiley face there?? Get some humor back into your life.

### Post by ALIENDUDE5300 on 2009-09-25
Before you start flaming me, take some time to actually read this post. Everything in this post is true, and not made up. I actually contacted Dell Customer Support and have chat logs to back up my claims. Before I begin, I would like to mention that I think that this is ridiculous for Dell to charge for Ubuntu, I am an avid Ubuntu user, I have been Using Linux since Slackware 10, and I have **NO** plans on ever using Windows (or Mac, although much more likely than Windows) as my primary operating system. I will be comparing the price and features of two Dell brand laptops with the exact same name, the "Studio XPS 13". Both versions (Windows and Ubuntu) are *exactly* $949 Now, let's begin comparing the "Features" of each computer... all this information is taken directly from the Dell website, and I even got a Dell Customer Support representative,  "Angelica M." to verify that all the information I am talking about is correct. I will be using quotes from her to back up my claims. Now then, other than the price being exactly the same for both operating systems, the configuration looks very similar. Both machines come with a 2Ghz Intel® Core™ 2 Duo P7350 processor, an "Edge-to-Edge 13.3" HD WXGA LCD" with a 2.0 Megapixel Camera integrated, a Slot Loading DVD+/-RW drive, 3GB DDR3 SDRAM memory clocked at 1067MHz, 250GB 7200 RPM SATA Hard Drives, an NVIDIA® GeForce® 9400M G graphics card (on board), identical sound chips, 56 WHr 6-cell Lithium Ion batteries, 1Yr Ltd Hardware Warranty (not really needed. ever.), and the exact same chassis and color. There are however, some differences that matter a lot, and they make it worth it to choose the Windows version of the laptop. First, the Windows version of the laptop obviously includes Microsoft Windows Vista Home Premium Edition (64-Bit), which may be needed to legally run certain programs that require a windows license under wine, not to mention a FREE update to Windows 7 when it comes out. This is particularly useful if you dual boot your computer. According to Amazon.com, [Vista Home Premium (64-Bit)]("http://www.amazon.com/Microsoft-Windows-Premium-64-bit-English/dp/B000MFIPDC/ref=sr_1_1?ie=UTF8&s=software&qid=1253922285&sr=8-1") is valued at $99.99, and [Windows 7 Home Premium Upgrade]("http://www.amazon.com/Microsoft-Windows-Home-Premium-Upgrade/dp/B002DHLUWK/ref=sr_1_1?ie=UTF8&s=software&qid=1253922342&sr=1-1") (NOT the OEM install version -- that's $80 more!) is valued at $119.99. That SHOULD make the Ubuntu version at least $100 less, if not the full $200 that is supposedly what Windows is valued at (I'd personally value it at around $20 max, but that's another story). In addition to paying the same price to not get an operating system (Ubuntu is free, you can install it on the PC after buying it with windows at the cost of a CD-R -- $19.99 for a [spindle of 100 52X Speed 700MB CD-Rs]("http://www.amazon.com/Verbatim-94554-CD-R-100-Disc-Spindle/dp/B00029U1DU/ref=sr_1_1?ie=UTF8&s=electronics&qid=1253922605&sr=8-1"), that's $0.19 to burn Ubuntu. It could even cost you nothing if you get a CD free from a friend. Installation is VERY easy, so that shouldn't be why you have to pay a premium for Ubuntu). Not only do you not get Windows Vista and 7, you also will get ripped of on your wireless adapter. Instead of the fancy "Dell Wireless 1510 802.11n Half Mini-Card" that comes with the Windows version, if you choose to go with Ubuntu, you get stuck with the not-so-great "Dell Wireless 1397 802.11a/g Half Mini-Card". This may look like it's just a different model number to non-technical users, but it is a HUGE deal. They may have degraded the wireless hardware due to incompatibility with Linux Wireless drivers, but that is NOT an excuse, as they are the manufacturer of the chip, and they had no problem making a driver for Windows. I decided to investigate the issue further. The following is from the chat me and Angelica had. The format of the chat was slightly modified, as what I copied and pasted looked bad compared to how I modified it, due to the extensive use of images in the chat, and the lack of an easy way to tell who said what without the colored text that was present during the live chat, but the actual words that were said are not in any way modified other than me removing personal information and insignificant details. Also, the representative's name was shortened from "BE_PR1_Rep_Angelica_M" to "Angelica", and my name was replaced with "Me". In addition to that, discussion spanning multiple lines from the same person was shortened to a single line, and timestamps were removed.

**Angelica**: Verified in website that both OS are being sold at same price. You can check online offers on Studio XPS 13 to compare.

**Angelica**: No discount.
**Me**: The only difference that I can see is that the Windows machine at $945, which is the exact same price as the Ubuntu machine seems to have a "Dell Wireless 1510 802.11n Half Mini-Card" instead of a "Dell Wireless 1397 802.11a/g Half Mini-Card". What is the difference between these models? It would seem that the Windows version of the same laptop just has a newer hardware revision that also supports Wireless N-Draft instead of just a and g. Other than that, everything is the same.
**Angelica**: Wireless N is compatible to all types of network and has 5x the speed and range than an average wireless card (a/b or g).
**Me**: Ok, well thanks for answering my questions. I might end up buying a system later when the Ubuntu systems are a bit cheaper.
**System:** The session has ended!

So there you have it.... Windows and Ubuntu both "cost" the same amount, but you get significantly better hardware and the added benefit of having a legally owned copy of Microsoft Windows if you choose the Windows computer over the Ubuntu one. :(

### Post by unutbu on 2009-10-03
Saving the post to a file, and running
```
sed -e 's/\. /.\n\n/g' file
```
will display the file with two returns after every sentence.

PS. I agree with your request, earthpigg, but it's nice to know there's another way to do it.

### Post by RedRat on 2009-10-03
This discussion about getting a "discount" from Dell because you buy an Ubuntu machine, at least in my opinion, out of line. Keep in mind that Dell does still provide Ubuntu hot line service phone service. Hey, these people do not work for free.

Also, keep in mind that the Windows machines, even if they are identical to the Ubuntu machines come with pre-installed software from various sources that they pay Dell to put on them. This in effect is a Windows subsidy against the cost of the machine. So even if Ubuntu is "free" and Windows costs Dell money, these costs can be balanced out. 

Debating about the relative costs of pre-offered software subsidies and costs of Windows licenses charge to Dell is a waste of time since we are not privy to Dell's accounting practices. Further, Dell offers their own online forum support at Dell. Again, those working those lines do not work for free. 

Frankly, it all comes down to this: Do you think the price of the ubuntu machine is the right one for you, regardless of what an equivalent windows machine costs? For me, the answer was yes, I thought the price for my 530n was fair and like the machine.

There are however, some differences that matter a lot, and they make it worth it to choose the Windows version of the laptop. First, the Windows version of the laptop obviously includes Microsoft Windows Vista Home Premium Edition (64-Bit), which may be needed to legally run certain programs that require a windows license under wine, not to mention a FREE update to Windows 7 when it comes out. This is particularly useful if you dual boot your computer. According to Amazon.com, [Vista Home Premium (64-Bit)]("http://www.amazon.com/Microsoft-Windows-Premium-64-bit-English/dp/B000MFIPDC/ref=sr_1_1?ie=UTF8&s=software&qid=1253922285&sr=8-1") is valued at $99.99, and [Windows 7 Home Premium Upgrade]("http://www.amazon.com/Microsoft-Windows-Home-Premium-Upgrade/dp/B002DHLUWK/ref=sr_1_1?ie=UTF8&s=software&qid=1253922342&sr=1-1") (NOT the OEM install version -- that's $80 more!) is valued at $119.99. That SHOULD make the Ubuntu version at least $100 less, if not the full $200 that is supposedly what Windows is valued at (I'd personally value it at around $20 max, but that's another story). In addition to paying the same price to not get an operating system (Ubuntu is free, you can install it on the PC after buying it with windows at the cost of a CD-R -- $19.99 for a [spindle of 100 52X Speed 700MB CD-Rs]("http://www.amazon.com/Verbatim-94554-CD-R-100-Disc-Spindle/dp/B00029U1DU/ref=sr_1_1?ie=UTF8&s=electronics&qid=1253922605&sr=8-1"), that's $0.19 to burn Ubuntu. The first question here is what is the real value of the Windows Vista Home Premium / 7 license? A full retail Windows Vista license with the upgrade to Windows 7 can be found in the US for about $160 better if one shops around. I use the United States since the OP of the thread before merger is in the US. A OEM version can go for $70 or lower. The differences are very significant [http://arstechnica.com/hardware/news/2007/01/8730.ars]("http://arstechnica.com/hardware/news/2007/01/8730.ars") and are particularly important for a GNU / Linux user who occasionally wants to use Windows or parts thereof. 

The Dell version of OEM Windows is further devalued by the addition of all sorts of trail software is may be in some cases very tricky to remove. The placement of this software is nothing more than ads for which Dell gets paid. Furthermore there are many people who would gladly pay $100 or more to get rid of this software and be left with a clean Windows installation. So for the sake of Argument I will give the Windows OS pre-installed by Dell a value of -$30

On the Ubuntu side one gets a clean version of Ubuntu with the addition of DVD playback (the latter is very important to a user in the United States the land of very oppressive IP laws) Lets say $20 for the DVD (legal in the US) playback 

On the software side the Ubuntu machine is ahead by $50 and that does not take into consideration that Ubuntu is a far superior operating system to Windows. Is this worth the difference in the Wireless card? The answer is yes. 

The Ubuntu machine is by far the better deal and if one wants Windows for whatever reason buy the full retail version. It will be far cheaper in the long run than the OEM version from a major manufacturer and well worth the difference in initial price

### Post by Cerin on 2009-10-29
What are your thoughts on Dell's commitment to offering Linux on it's machines? Is it authentic?

When I bought a Dell Mini 9 last year, preloaded with Ubuntu, I thought Dell was serious about supporting Linux. I still use the Mini, and have found it problem free.

Now I'm not so sure. I was in the market for a full-blown desktop replacement Linux laptop, and saw an ad for Dell's line of preloaded Linux machines at [http://www.dell.com/content/topics/segtopic.aspx/ubuntu?c=us&l=en&cs=19](http://www.dell.com/content/topics/segtopic.aspx/ubuntu?c=us&l=en&cs=19)

The first thing that irritated me was the note at the top of the page that warns the visitor that it's not Windows, and provides a link to shop for Windows machines. Seems a strange way to promote Ubuntu, but fair enough. Linux is still somewhat unknown outside of tech circles, so I can't completely blame Dell for hand-holding potentially clueless customers.

I move on and click "Shop for Ubuntu". The Studio XPS 13 seems like the best desktop replacement candidate, so I click "choose" so see the full detail page. Again, I'm a little irritated to see the top of the page contains a Windows ad, "Windows®. Life without WallsTM. Dell recommends Windows 7." In other words, Dell recommends you not buy what you're thinking of buying.

Still, for $1,059, it doesn't seem like a bad price. However, just out of curiosity, I check out the equivalent configuration for the Windows system at [http://www.dell.com/us/en/home/notebooks/laptop-studio-xps-13/pd.aspx?refid=laptop-studio-xps-13&cs=19&s=dhs](http://www.dell.com/us/en/home/notebooks/laptop-studio-xps-13/pd.aspx?refid=laptop-studio-xps-13&cs=19&s=dhs)

I wish I can say I was surprised, but I found the lowest-end Studio XPS 13 Windows configuration had a cheaper base price of $999, had a faster processor (P7450  verses P7350 in the Ubuntu config) and had a slightly better wireless card (1510 802.11n verses 1397 802.11a/g in the Ubuntu config). All other specs looked the same.

So to recap, no Windows, a less powerful processor, an older wireless card, and the Ubuntu configuration is still $60 *more* expensive than the nearest Windows configuration? Am I missing something here? What exactly am I paying for in the Ubuntu configuration?

Is Dell simply not serious about offering Ubuntu, or are they just not good at this whole "marketing" thing? I really want to support a company that supports Linux, but I'm not going to pay more for less.

### Post by Cerin on 2009-10-29
No offense, but I don't buy that argument. The economies of scale only apply if there's differing amounts of overhead involved for each selection. 

Dell doesn't manufacturer either Windows or Ubuntu. The physical act of writing ISO A (containing Ubuntu) costs essentially the same amount of electricity as writing ISO B (containing Windows).

You might have a point if you take into account the cost of testing Ubuntu compatibility with their hardware. But still, you'd think the lack of the Windows licensing cost would make up the difference.

If I were the paranoid type, I'd say Microsoft was simply up to their old tricks of cutting manufacturers a discount as long as they keep giving Linux second class treatment.

### Post by Miguel on 2009-10-29
> **snowpine said:**

It is sleazy because Windows is a monopoly. No, it's not a high-school example of monopoly. It is, however, a real world example of monopoly. Thus, you can afford offer a huge discount and sell at a loss for a time to push the competition out of the market just because you have such a huge market share.

This is a similar reason why many people in the EU are crazy with the South Korean subsidies given to ship makers.

### Post by aysiu on 2009-10-29
> **Miguel said:**
> It is sleazy because Windows is a monopoly. No, it's not a high-school example of monopoly. It is, however, a real world example of monopoly. Thus, you can afford offer a huge discount and sell at a loss for a time to push the competition out of the market just because you have such a huge market share.

This is a similar reason why many people in the EU are crazy with the South Korean subsidies given to ship makers.
It's the same reason Wal-Mart is sleazy for offering lower prices than its competitors.

Yes, consumers don't complain about low prices, but many of Wal-Mart's suppliers struggle to stay in business because they're in a jam: if they refuse to offer their products at such a low price to Wal-Mart, they can barely make a profit on those products, but if they cut Wal-Mart out altogether, they can't move enough units to make a profit either.

Dell knows they cannot survive in their current state if they piss of Microsoft, but offering deals on Windows computers continually makes their profit margins razor thin.

> No, you're sleazy if you sell Billy's apple pies for $9 and Lenny's apple pies for $15, even though for all intents and purposes they taste exactly the same, but you like Billy more than Lenny, because Billy's giving you $6 ontop of each of his pies you sell.

Um, okay... if Billy is giving me, the middleman, a $6 discount on each pie, wouldn't it be much, much "sleazier" to pocket the $6 instead of passing the savings on to the consumer??? ;)

"Food that tastes the same needs to cost the same" is a ridiculous argument. The same applesauce made in the same factory from apples grown on the same tree might cost ten times more per unit packaged as a Motts Kids Meal Snack Pack as compared to a big jar of generic brand. (And that's assuming Windows and Ubuntu taste the same, which as we all know, they don't!)

My head is starting to hurt from all the mixed metaphors... buy whatever computer you want, it's all good. ;)

---

### Post by snowpine on 2009-10-29
> **aysiu said:**
> Why does that analogy make sense? Are apples sold by a monopoly? Are cherries free?

Does the consumer need to know/understand why some things cost more than others? Are businesses in a capitalist society allowed to set their prices based on a variety of internal and external factors?

Maybe my apple pies are cheaper than cherry because I have a warehouse full of apples. Maybe they're cheaper because I'm buddies with the apple grower. Maybe they're cheaper because they are more popular and I can therefore bake them in bigger batches. Maybe they're cheaper because I'm trying to create a luxury branding for the cherry pies. Maybe they're cheaper because I said so and if you don't like it you can shop someplace else. ;)

### Post by Cerin on 2009-10-29
> **snowpine said:**
> Um, okay... if Billy is giving me, the middleman, a $6 discount on each pie, wouldn't it be much, much "sleazier" to pocket the $6 instead of passing the savings on to the consumer??? ;)

"Food that tastes the same needs to cost the same" is a ridiculous argument. The same applesauce made in the same factory from apples grown on the same tree might cost ten times more per unit packaged as a Motts Kids Meal Snack Pack as compared to a big jar of generic brand. (And that's assuming Windows and Ubuntu taste the same, which as we all know, they don't!)

My head is starting to hurt from all the mixed metaphors... buy whatever computer you want, it's all good. ;)

If Billy has a near-monopoly on apple pies, then this action is illegal under anti-trust law.

He's selling at a loss, so that his competitor will go bust, and then he'll jack his price back up to $15. So yes, you and the customer are saving $6 in the short term, but you're both losing in the long-term. That's what anti-trust regulation is designed to prevent.

---

Right, I understand that Microsoft has come under legal scrutiny for potentially breaking various anti-trust laws on various occasions... but tell me again how exactly does that make Dell "sleazy"?

Maybe my apple pies are cheaper than cherry because I have a warehouse full of apples. Maybe they're cheaper because I'm buddies with the apple grower. Maybe they're cheaper because they are more popular and I can therefore bake them in bigger batches. Maybe they're cheaper because I'm trying to create a luxury branding for the cherry pies. Ok, now I'm starting to think you're simply trolling.

First, no society is 100% capitalist. Granted, businesses have tremendous leeway to set prices (as they should), but there are always laws and regulations that limit pricing in certain scenarios.

Besides, as the previous poster noted, your analogy doesn't make sense. Comparing Windows to Linux isn't apples to oranges (or cherries). They're similar products performing nearly identical tasks on virtually identical hardware. A better analogy is apples to apples.

### Post by RedRat on 2009-10-29
Well this whole discussion of cost and what Dell charges has been talked about here in these forum for months now. All must recognize that along with the the Windows machines comes all the crapware that Dell is paid to pre-install! This lowers the final cost of any given machine. Now how much it is lowered, none of us know because we are not privy to Dell's accounting procedures, but my guess is that it is considerable. 

Also keep in mind that Dell does have a Linux support group and web page. They probably also work out the engineering of ensuring that Linux machines all have compatible software. These guys are being Paid, folks! they don't work for free. Does MS subsidize Dell for servicing for software problems, more than likely they do. 

I bought my Linux Dell 530n a couple of years ago and I must say that it worked right out of the box. Now there might be some geeks out there that dearly love to fiddle with the OS and love problem solving. For me, the computer is a tool that allows me to surf the web, email, and write. I want trouble free! I detest the problems with Linux's handling of graphic cards, this is perhaps the most severe annoyance that I have found. Hopefully, with the release of 9.10 and 10.4, maybe they have gotten past that--I hope so. But to me, it is worth the extra cost, if there really is an extra cost, to get the pre-loaded Linux machines from Dell, or perhaps I might look into HP.
